Objections and Answers to the Proof on Motion
A series of five objections and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the proof on motion and how the author refutes each one.

Core Idea
The author systematically answers each objection, revealing the deeper nature of physical and celestial powers.

This passage presents a series of objections to the earlier proof about motion, followed by detailed answers. The objections range from celestial motions to the nature of starting points and speeds. The answers clarify the scope of the proof and the differences between physical and volitional powers.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and answers as a dialogue
Read the entire passage once without pausing to get the flow of the argument. Then go back and study each objection and its corresponding answer.
2
Identify the main objections
There is a general objection about celestial motions and four detailed objections. Notice how each is introduced with 'ولو سلم'.
The detailed objections cover: eternal powers, starting point difference, speed difference, and quantity difference with the moon example.
3
Follow each answer and see how it addresses the objection
The answers are numbered: answer to the general objection, to the second and third together, to the fourth, to the fifth, and finally a separate answer to the original proof.
Some objections are answered together.
4
Reflect on the final answer
The final answer denies the proportionality assumption by claiming that powers may be indivisible accidents, like unity or paternity.
This is a key point in the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن نوقض الدليل إجمالاً بالحركات الفلكية، فإنها مع عدم تناهيها عندهم مستندة إلى قوى جسمانية لها إدراكات جزئية. إذ التعقل الكلي لا يكفي في جزئيات الحركة على ما سيجيء. وتفصيلاً بأنه لم لا يجوز أن تكون القوى الجسمانية أزلية لا يكون لحركاتها مبدأ؟ ولو سلم فإنا لا نسلم إمكان ما فرضتم من اتحاد المبدأ، بل مبدأ حركة الأصغر أصغر من مبدأ حركة الأكبر. ولو سلم فلم لا يجوز أن يكون التفاوت الذي لابد منه هو التفاوت بالسرعة والبطء بأن يكون حركة الأصغر أسرع في القسرية، وأبطأ في الطبيعية من غير انقطاع. ولو سلم فالتفاوت بالزيادة والنقصان لا يوجب الانقطاع كما إذا فرضنا لحركة فلك القمر، وفلك زحل مبدأ من موازاة نقطة معينة من الفلك الأعظم، فإن دورات القمر أضعاف دورات زحل مع عدم تناهيهما. أجيب عن الأول : بأن حركات الأفلاك إرادية مستندة إلى إرادات وتعقلات جزئية مستندة إلى نفوسها المجردة في ذواتها المقارنة في أفعالها بالمادة المدركة للجزئيات بواسطة الآلات، وكلامنا في تأثير القوى الحالة في الأجسام. وعن الثاني والثالث : بأن فرض المبدأ الواحد للحركتين بأن يعتبرا من نقطة واحدة من أوساط المسافة يماسها الطرف الذي يليها كاف في إثبات المطلوب، ولا خفاء في إمكانه. وإن لم يكن للحركة بداية وليس المراد بالمبدأ مجموع حيز الجسم حتى يكون مبدأ حركة الجسم الأصغر أصغر. وعن الرابع : بأن الاختلاف بالسرعة والبطء يكون تفاوتاً بحسب الشدة. وليس الكلام فيه، بل في التفاوت بحسب المدة والعدة، ومعناه الزيادة والنقصان في زمان الحركة وعددها. وعن الخامس : بأن دورات القمر أو زحل ليست جملة موجودة يمكن الحكم عليها بالزيادة والنقصان، ولا هناك أيضاً قوة موجودة تستند تلك الدورات إليها، بل إنما تستند إلى إرادات متجددة متعددة متعاقبة لا توجد إلا مع الحركات بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإن كون جملة الأفعال، وإن لم تكن حاصلة في الحال لكن كون القوة قوية عليها أمر حاصل في الحال متفاوت بالزيادة والنقصان بالنسبة إلى تحريك الصغير والكبير وفي هذا نظر، وعليه زيادة كلام يذكر في إبطال التسلسل. وأجيب عن أصل الدليل بعد تسليم تأثير القوى بأن ما ذكرتم من اختلاف القسرية باختلاف القابل والطبيعة باختلاف الفاعل بحيث يكون تفاوت القوة على المعاوقة، أو على التحريك في الجسم الصغير والكبير بنسبة مقداريهما حتى لو كان مقدار الصغير نصف مقدار الكبير، كانت قوة معاوقته أو تحريكه نصف قوة معاوقة الكبير أو تحريكه ليلزم أن تكون حركته القسرية ضعف حركة الكبير وحركته الطبيعية نصفها ممنوع، لجواز أن تكون القوة من الأعراض التي لا تنقسم بانقسام المحل كالوحدة والنقطة والأبوة.
If the proof is refuted in general by celestial motions, for they, although unlimited in their view, are based on physical powers that have partial perceptions. For universal intellection is not sufficient for particulars of motion as will come. And in detail: why is it not permissible that physical powers be eternal without a beginning for their motions? And if conceded, we do not concede the possibility of what you assumed of the unity of the starting point; rather, the starting point of the smaller motion is smaller than the starting point of the larger motion. And if conceded, why is it not permissible that the necessary difference be the difference in speed and slowness, so that the motion of the smaller is faster in forced and slower in natural without cessation? And if conceded, the difference by increase and decrease does not necessitate cessation, as if we suppose for the motion of the moon's sphere and the sphere of Saturn a starting point from alignment with a particular point of the highest sphere, for the revolutions of the moon are multiples of the revolutions of Saturn without either being finite. The first is answered: that the celestial motions are volitional, based on volitions and partial intellections based on their abstract souls, which in themselves are connected in their actions with matter that perceives particulars through instruments, and our speech concerns the influence of powers inherent in bodies. And the second and third are answered: that the assumption of a single starting point for the two motions, by considering them from one point from the middle of the distance that the adjacent side touches, suffices to establish what is sought, and there is no obscurity in its possibility, even if the motion has no beginning, and the intended meaning of 'starting point' is not the total space of the body so that the starting point of the smaller body's motion is smaller. And the fourth is answered: that the difference in speed and slowness is a difference in intensity, and the discussion is not about that, but about the difference in duration and count, and its meaning is the increase and decrease in the time of motion and its number. And the fifth is answered: that the revolutions of the moon or Saturn are not an existing totality upon which increase and decrease can be judged, nor is there there an existing power to which those revolutions are attributed; rather, they are attributed to renewed, multiple, successive volitions that do not exist except with the motions, unlike our case, for the fact that the totality of actions, even if not present at the moment, but the fact that the power is strong over them is a matter present at the moment, varying by increase and decrease relative to moving the small and the large. And in this there is consideration, and upon it there is additional speech mentioned in the refutation of infinite regress. And the original proof was answered after granting the influence of powers: that what you mentioned of the difference of forced by difference of receiver and natural by difference of agent, such that the power's strength in resistance or in movement in the small and large body is proportional to their magnitudes, so that if the small's magnitude is half the large's, its power of resistance or movement would be half the large's power, necessitating that its forced motion be double that of the large and its natural motion half — this is denied, because it is possible that the power is among the accidents that are not divided by the division of the locus, like unity, point, and paternity.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections to the earlier proof about motion. The first objection is general: celestial motions are infinite and based on physical powers with partial perceptions, so they seem to contradict the proof. Then detailed objections follow: 1) Physical powers could be eternal without a beginning. 2) The starting points for small and large motions cannot be the same. 3) The difference could be in speed rather than cessation. 4) Difference in quantity does not necessitate cessation, as seen in planetary orbits. The author answers each: 1) Celestial motions are volitional, not purely physical, so they do not apply. 2&3) Assuming a single starting point from the middle of the distance is sufficient, even if no beginning exists. 4) Speed difference is intensity, not duration. 5) The total cycles are not a single existing set, and the power is renewed with each motion, unlike the original case where a single power supports multiple actions. The final answer after granting the premise denies the proportionality claim because powers might be indivisible attributes like unity, point, or paternity.

الحركات الفلكية
القوى الجسمانية
الإرادة
تجدد
أعراض لا تنقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objections are unanswerable and disprove the proof.
Correction: Each objection is carefully refuted, showing the proof's strength.

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the objection about celestial motions?
A) They are finite.
B) They are volitional.
C) They are based on partial perceptions.
D) They are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The proof on motion is defended by distinguishing between physical and volitional powers and by denying that powers are divisible.

?
The passage argues that powers may be indivisible like unity or point. Reflect on your own sense of self: is your 'power' (will, strength) divisible or a unified whole from Allah? How does this affect your trust in Him? Are you willing to admit that your abilities are not independently divisible but are a single gift from Him?
?
If you believe that all events have material causes, how does the author's distinction between forced and natural motions (based on receiver vs agent) expose your negligence of the Creator's direct involvement in every moment? Will you begin to attribute every action and perception directly to Allah, not to inert forces?

A Single Starting Point for Two Motions
Response to objections 2 and 3 about a single starting point

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the objection that two motions cannot share a single starting point without division.

Core Idea
A single starting point can serve two motions from the middle.

The second and third objections claimed that if two motions originate from the same point, that point must be divided. The response shows that by considering a single point in the middle of the distance, both motions can proceed without requiring division. This subtle argument relies on the concept that the starting point is not the total space of the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage carefully. Notice the key phrase 'بأن فرض المبدأ الواحد للحركتين'.
2
Understand the role of the middle
The response proposes taking one point from the middle of the distance. Both motions touch this point, so a single point suffices.
3
Consider the case of no beginning
Even if motion has no beginning, this solution still works because we are not requiring an absolute starting point.
The objection may assume a beginning; this response shows it's not necessary.
4
Clarify 'mabda'' (starting point)
The starting point is not the entire space of the body, so it does not have to be smaller for a smaller body.
This refutes the objection's implicit assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالمادة المدركة للجزئيات بواسطة الآلات وكلامتا في تأثير القوى الحالة في الأجسام. وعن الثاني والثالث: بأن فرض المبدأ الواحد للحركتين بأن يعتبرا من نقطة واحدة من أوساط المسافة يماسها الطرف الذي يليها كاف في إثبات المطلوب، ولأخفاء في إمكانه. وإن لم يكن للحركة بداية وليس المراد بالمبدأ مجموع حيز الجسم حتى يكون مبدأ حركة الجسم الأصغر أصغر. وعن الرابع: بأن الاختلاف بالسرعة والبطء يكون تفاوتاً بحسب الشدة. وليس الكلام فيه، بل في التفاوت بحسب المدة والعدة، ومعناه الزيادة والنقصان في زمان الحركة وعددها. وعن الخامس: بأن دورات القمر أو زحل ليست جملة موجودة يمكن الحكم عليها بالزيادة والنقصان، ولا هناك أيضاً قوة موجودة تستند تلك الدورات إليها، بل إنما تستند إلى إرادات متجددة متعددة متعاقبة لا توجد إلا مع الحركات بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإن كون جملة الأفعال، وإن لم تكن حاصلة في الحال لكن كون القوة قوية عليها أمر حاصل في الحال متفاوت بالزيادة والنقصان بالنسبة إلى تحريك الصغير والكبير وفي هذا نظر، وعليه زيادة كلام يذكر في إبطال التسلسل. وأجيب عن أصل الدليل بعد تسليم تأثير القوى بأن ما ذكرتم من اختلاف القسرية باختلاف القابل والطبيعة باختلاف الفاعل بحيث يكون تفاوت القوة على المعاوقة، أو على التحريك في الجسم الصغير والكبير بنسبة مقداريهما حتى لو كان مقدار الصغير نصف مقدار الكبير، كانت قوة معاوقته أو تحريكه نصف قوة معاوقة الكبير أو تحريكه ليلزم أن تكون حركته القسرية ضعف حركة الكبير وحركته الطبيعية نصفها ممنوع، لجواز أن تكون القوة من الأعراض التي لا تنقسم بانقسام المحل كالوحدة والنقطة والأبوة.
By the matter perceived by the instruments and our speech concerning the effect of the powers inherent in bodies. And concerning the second and third [objection]: that postulating a single starting point for two motions by considering them from one point in the middle of the distance that the terminal end touches is sufficient to prove what is sought, and hiddenness in its possibility. Even if there is no beginning for motion, and what is meant by the starting point is not the total space of the body such that the starting point of the motion of the smaller body would be smaller. And concerning the fourth: that difference in speed and slowness is a variation according to intensity. And the discussion is not about that, but about variation according to duration and number, meaning increase and decrease in time of motion and its number. And concerning the fifth: that the revolutions of the moon or Saturn are not an existing totality that can be judged with increase and decrease, nor is there an existing power to which those revolutions are attributed; rather, they are attributed to renewed, multiple, successive wills that exist only with the motions, unlike what we are discussing. For the fact that the totality of acts, even if they are not actually present, yet the power being powerful over them is something actually present, varying in increase and decrease with respect to moving the small and the large. And there is consideration in this, and upon it additional speech mentioned in refuting infinite regress. And an answer was given to the original proof after conceding the effect of powers: that what you mentioned of difference in coercion according to the recipient and nature according to the agent, such that the variation of power over resistance or over movement in the small and large body is proportional to their amounts, so that if the amount of the small is half the amount of the large, then its power of resistance or movement would be half the power of resistance or movement of the large, necessitating that its forced motion would be double the motion of the large and its natural motion half of it, is denied. Because it is possible that power is among the accidents that are not divisible by the division of the subject, like unity, the point, and paternity.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents five responses to objections concerning the effect of powers (quwa) in bodies. The first response (to the second and third objections) argues that postulating a single starting point for two motions from a point in the middle is sufficient, even if the motion has no beginning. The second response (to the fourth objection) distinguishes between variation based on intensity (shidda) and variation based on duration/ number; the objection mistakenly focuses on the former while the issue is the latter. The third response (to the fifth objection) contrasts celestial revolutions (like those of the moon or Saturn) with human actions: celestial revolutions are not a totality existing at once and are caused by successive wills, whereas human power (quwwa) over a totality of acts can be present at once and can vary. The fourth response (to the original proof) denies that power necessarily divides proportionally with the body, because power may be an indivisible accident (like unity or the point). Thus the objection's conclusion is invalid.

Key Terms
مبدأ (mabda')
حركة (haraka)
وسط (wasat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) A single point in the middle can serve as a common starting point
D) The starting point must be the total space of the body

Key Takeaway
A single point in the middle can serve as a common starting point for two motions without division.

Intensity vs. Duration in Motion
Response to objection 4 on speed and slowness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between variation in speed due to intensity and variation due to duration, and why the objection confuses them.

Core Idea
Variation in speed is about intensity, not duration or number.

The fourth objection claimed that variations in speed and slowness disprove the argument. The response clarifies that the objection mistakenly focuses on variation due to intensity (shidda), whereas the real issue is variation in duration and number of motions. This distinction is crucial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the contrast between 'الشدة' and 'المدة والعدة'.
2
Understand the two types of variation
Variation in speed can be by intensity (like faster/slower in a given time) or by duration/number (how long or how many motions).
The objection was about intensity, but the discussion is about duration.
3
Recognize the refutation
The argument about powers is not about how fast a motion is in a moment, but about the capacity to produce motions over time.

This passage presents five responses to objections concerning the effect of powers (quwa) in bodies. Have you ever made excuses for not following through on a commitment, telling yourself you 'just don't have the same drive' as others? Could it be that the real issue is not your capacity but the number of times you actually try? Identify one area where you've given up after one intense attempt, and commit to trying again—and again—starting tomorrow.
?
This lesson teaches that variation in duration and number is what matters for evaluating actions, not the intensity of a single effort. Where in your life have you been fooled by the allure of a 'fast start' while neglecting patient persistence? What concrete step will you take today to prioritize steady action over dramatic bursts?

Celestial Revolutions vs. Human Powers
Response to objection 5 on celestial motions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the objection based on celestial revolutions, and why they are not comparable to human actions.

Core Idea
Celestial motions depend on successive wills, not a single power.

The fifth objection cites the revolutions of the moon or Saturn as examples where power varies. The response argues that these revolutions are not a totality present at once; they are caused by successive, renewed wills that exist only with the motions. In contrast, human power over a set of actions exists all at once and can vary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the contrast between 'جملة موجودة' and 'إرادات متجددة'.
2
Understand the celestial case
The moon and Saturn have revolutions that are not a single existing totality. They are produced by multiple wills that each exist only at the time of motion.
3
Compare with human power
In our case, the power is present at once and can be stronger or weaker relative to moving small or large objects.
4
Note the consideration
The author acknowledges there is consideration in this and refers to additional discussion on refuting infinite regress.
This hints at a deeper debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالمادة المدركة للجزئيات بواسطة الآلات وكلامتا في تأثير القوى الحالة في الأجسام. وعن الثاني والثالث: بأن فرض المبدأ الواحد للحركتين بأن يعتبرا من نقطة واحدة من أوساط المسافة يماسها الطرف الذي يليها كاف في إثبات المطلوب، ولأخفاء في إمكانه. وإن لم يكن للحركة بداية وليس المراد بالمبدأ مجموع حيز الجسم حتى يكون مبدأ حركة الجسم الأصغر أصغر. وعن الرابع: بأن الاختلاف بالسرعة والبطء يكون تفاوتاً بحسب الشدة. وليس الكلام فيه، بل في التفاوت بحسب المدة والعدة، ومعناه الزيادة والنقصان في زمان الحركة وعددها. وعن الخامس: بأن دورات القمر أو زحل ليست جملة موجودة يمكن الحكم عليها بالزيادة والنقصان، ولا هناك أيضاً قوة موجودة تستند تلك الدورات إليها، بل إنما تستند إلى إرادات متجددة متعددة متعاقبة لا توجد إلا مع الحركات بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإن كون جملة الأفعال، وإن لم تكن حاصلة في الحال لكن كون القوة قوية عليها أمر حاصل في الحال متفاوت بالزيادة والنقصان بالنسبة إلى تحريك الصغير والكبير وفي هذا نظر، وعليه زيادة كلام يذكر في إبطال التسلسل. وأجيب عن أصل الدليل بعد تسليم تأثير القوى بأن ما ذكرتم من اختلاف القسرية باختلاف القابل والطبيعة باختلاف الفاعل بحيث يكون تفاوت القوة على المعاوقة، أو على التحريك في الجسم الصغير والكبير بنسبة مقداريهما حتى لو كان مقدار الصغير نصف مقدار الكبير، كانت قوة معاوقته أو تحريكه نصف قوة معاوقة الكبير أو تحريكه ليلزم أن تكون حركته القسرية ضعف حركة الكبير وحركته الطبيعية نصفها ممنوع، لجواز أن تكون القوة من الأعراض التي لا تنقسم بانقسام المحل كالوحدة والنقطة والأبوة.
By the matter perceived by the instruments and our speech concerning the effect of the powers inherent in bodies. And concerning the second and third [objection]: that postulating a single starting point for two motions by considering them from one point in the middle of the distance that the terminal end touches is sufficient to prove what is sought, and hiddenness in its possibility. Even if there is no beginning for motion, and what is meant by the starting point is not the total space of the body such that the starting point of the motion of the smaller body would be smaller. And concerning the fourth: that difference in speed and slowness is a variation according to intensity. And the discussion is not about that, but about variation according to duration and number, meaning increase and decrease in time of motion and its number. And concerning the fifth: that the revolutions of the moon or Saturn are not an existing totality that can be judged with increase and decrease, nor is there an existing power to which those revolutions are attributed; rather, they are attributed to renewed, multiple, successive wills that exist only with the motions, unlike what we are discussing. For the fact that the totality of acts, even if they are not actually present, yet the power being powerful over them is something actually present, varying in increase and decrease with respect to moving the small and the large. And there is consideration in this, and upon it additional speech mentioned in refuting infinite regress. And an answer was given to the original proof after conceding the effect of powers: that what you mentioned of difference in coercion according to the recipient and nature according to the agent, such that the variation of power over resistance or over movement in the small and large body is proportional to their amounts, so that if the amount of the small is half the amount of the large, then its power of resistance or movement would be half the power of resistance or movement of the large, necessitating that its forced motion would be double the motion of the large and its natural motion half of it, is denied. Because it is possible that power is among the accidents that are not divisible by the division of the subject, like unity, the point, and paternity.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents five responses to objections concerning the effect of powers (quwa) in bodies. The first response (to the second and third objections) argues that postulating a single starting point for two motions from a point in the middle is sufficient, even if the motion has no beginning. The second response (to the fourth objection) distinguishes between variation based on intensity (shidda) and variation based on duration/ number; the objection mistakenly focuses on the former while the issue is the latter. The third response (to the fifth objection) contrasts celestial revolutions (like those of the moon or Saturn) with human actions: celestial revolutions are not a totality existing at once and are caused by successive wills, whereas human power (quwwa) over a totality of acts can be present at once and can vary. The fourth response (to the original proof) denies that power necessarily divides proportionally with the body, because power may be an indivisible accident (like unity or the point). Thus the objection's conclusion is invalid.

دورة (dawra)
إرادة (irada)
قوة (quwwa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial revolutions show that power varies, just as in human actions.
Correction: Celestial revolutions are caused by multiple successive wills, not a single power present at once.

Checkpoint

A) A single existing power
B) A totality of acts present at once
C) Renewed, successive wills
D) A single will

Key Takeaway
Celestial revolutions depend on successive wills, while human power over acts is present at once.

?

?
The celestial bodies move with regularity through renewed wills each moment. In your life, do you rely on a single resolve to maintain consistency in worship, or do you renew your intention with each action? Which approach is more stable, and what does it say about your dependence on Allah?
?
If your power over future righteous acts is actually present now, then what responsibility do you have to act upon it? Knowing that your power can vary in strength, what will you do today to increase your capacity for obedience? Can you identify one area where you have been waiting for a future moment rather than using the power you already have?

Power as an Indivisible Accident
Response to the original proof on proportionality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the original proof that power necessarily divides proportionally with the body.

Core Idea
Some accidents, like power, may be indivisible.

The original proof argued that if power varied proportionally with body size, then forced and natural motions would have fixed ratios. The response denies that power must divide with the subject, because some accidents (like unity, point, paternity) are indivisible. Thus, the objection's conclusion does not follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the conditional: 'حتى لو كان مقدار الصغير نصف مقدار الكبير...'.
2
Understand the objection's logic
The objection assumed that power over resistance or movement is proportional to body size, leading to forced motion being double for small bodies.
3
Understand the response
The response denies this assumption, arguing that power may be an indivisible accident like unity or the point.
Indivisible accidents do not change with the size of the subject.
4
Apply the analogy
Just as a point is not divided if the body is divided, power may remain whole regardless of body size.

The Impossibility of Circular and Infinite Causal Chains
Defines and proves the impossibility of circular and infinite causal chains

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why circular causation and infinite regress in causal chains are rationally impossible according to Islamic theology.

Core Idea
A thing cannot depend on itself, nor can dependence go on forever.

This passage tackles two fundamental impossibilities in reasoning about causation: circular dependency (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul). Before we can build any proof about Allah or creation, we must accept that causal chains have a starting point. The author defines both problems, gives a clear proof against circularity, and answers a likely objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice how the author first defines the two types before proving one of them.
The passage is dense – take your time.
2
Identify the two impossibility claims
Find where the author states that circular causation (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) are impossible. Note the definitions: finite subjects (circle) vs. infinite subjects (chain).
The proof is only given for the circle here.
3
Analyze the proof and objection
Work through the proof for dawr: it would require a thing to precede itself. Then study the objection (a thing by its essence could be its own cause) and the response (the remote cause remains necessary).
This is the core logical argument.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this matters for theology: if causal chains could loop or go on forever, then there would be no First Cause (Allah). This argument establishes the need for a necessary being at the start.
This connects to later discussions about Allah's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس: يستحيل تراقي عروض العلية والمعلولية لا إلى نهاية سواء كان في معروضات متناهية ويسمى دوراً أو غير متناهية، تسلسلا. ويسمى . أما الأول: فلاستحالة تقدم الشيء على نفسه بالمعنى الذي يصح قولنا: وجدت حركة اليد، فوجدت حركة الخاتم بخلاف العكس. فإن قيل: تقدم الشيء على نفسه غير لازم، لأن المحتاج إلى الشيء لا يلزم أن يكون محتاجاً إلى ذلك الشيء، اذ العلة القريبة كافية، وإلا لزم التخلف، ولأن الشيء يجوز أن يكون بماهيته علة لما هو علة، لما هو علة لوجوده. قلنا: ما لم توجد البعيدة لم توجد القريبة وما لم توجد القريبة لم توجد المعلول، وهو معنى الاحتياج وما ذكر من كون الشيء بماهيته علة لما هو علة
The sixth investigation: The ascent of the occurrence of causality and effectness to no end is impossible, whether it is in finite subjects and is called a circle (dawr), or in infinite [subjects] and is called an infinite chain (tasalsul). And it is called... As for the first: it is due to the impossibility of a thing preceding itself in the sense that it is correct for us to say: the motion of the hand existed, then the motion of the ring existed, contrary to the reverse. For if it is said: the thing preceding itself is not necessary, because that which is in need of a thing does not necessarily need that thing, since the proximate cause is sufficient, otherwise there would be delay, and because it is permissible for a thing by its essence to be a cause for what is a cause for what is a cause for its existence. We say: as long as the remote [cause] does not exist, the proximate does not exist, and as long as the proximate does not exist, the effect does not exist. And that is the meaning of need. And what was mentioned of a thing by its essence being a cause for what is a cause
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the impossibility of circular causation (دور) and infinite regress (تسلسل). The author states that it is impossible for causality (علية) and effectness (معلولية) to ascend infinitely, whether the subjects are finite (resulting in a circle) or infinite (resulting in an infinite chain). The proof for the circle (دور) is given: it would entail a thing preceding itself, which is absurd. An example is given: the motion of the hand causing the motion of the ring; the reverse cannot occur. An objection is raised: a thing need not precede itself because the proximate cause is sufficient, and a thing can by its essence be a cause for something that is a cause for its own existence. The answer is that the remote cause is necessary for the proximate, and the proximate for the effect; that is the meaning of need. Thus the objection fails because the chain of dependence remains. The passage concludes by reiterating that the idea of a thing being a cause for its own cause is insufficient to avoid the regress.

Key Terms
علية (علة)
معلولية
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
تقدم الشيء على نفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A thing can be a cause for its own cause without any regress or circularity, because the proximate cause is sufficient.
Correction: The remote cause remains necessary for the existence of the proximate cause, and thus the dependence is not eliminated.

Checkpoint
What is the proof given against the possibility of a circle (دور) in causation?
A) It would lead to infinite regress
B) It would require a thing to precede itself
C) It would violate the principle of sufficient reason
D) It would require the subjects to be finite

Key Takeaway
Circular causation is impossible because it would require a thing to precede itself, and infinite regress is similarly impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If infinite regress is impossible, then your existence must have an ultimate source. Are you living in gratitude and obedience to that source, or are you acting as if you are self-sufficient? How can you concretely acknowledge your need for the First Cause today?

6 min
The Impossibility of Circular Causal Chains
Definition of circular and infinite causal regression and proof of circularity's impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the impossibility of circular causality and how it differs from infinite regress.

Core Idea
Circular causality is impossible because it makes a thing prior to itself.

This passage from Sanusi's al-Maqasid addresses a fundamental principle of causality: causal chains cannot loop back on themselves. Before we can discuss how things come into existence through causes, we must establish that causality cannot be circular. Sanusi defines two types of improper causal regression and provides a proof against circularity, along with a key objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of regression
Read the passage to grasp the definitions of circularity (دور) and infinite regress (تسلسل). Note that circularity involves finite subjects, while infinite regress is without end.
These are the two forms of 'تراقي' mentioned in the title.
2
Examine the proof against circularity
The proof argues that circularity requires a thing to be prior to itself, which is impossible. Sanusi illustrates with the example of hand and ring movements.
The example shows that causality is directional: hand movement causes ring movement, not vice versa.
3
Consider the objection and answer
An objection claims that the proximate cause might suffice, but Sanusi shows that without the remote cause, the effect cannot exist. This clarifies the necessity of non-circular causal chains.
The objection misunderstands that need is transitive: the effect depends on all causes in the chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس : يستحيل تراقي عروض العلية والمعلولية لا إلى نهاية سواء كان في معروضات متناهية ويسمى دوراً أو غير متناهية، تسلسلا. ويسمى . أما الأول : فلاستحالة تقدم الشيء على نفسه بالمعنى الذي يصح قولنا : (وجد فوجد على ما هو اللازم في العلية حيث يصح أن يقال : وجدت حركة اليد، فوجدت حركة الخاتم بخلاف العكس . فإن قيل : تقدم الشيء على نفسه غير لازم، لأن المحتاج إلى الشيء لا يلزم أن يكون محتاجاً إلى ذلك الشيء ، اذ العلة القريبة كافية، وإلا لزم التخلف، ولأن الشيء يجوز أن يكون بماهيته علة لما هو علة، لما هو علة لوجوده .قلنا ما لم توجد البعيدة لم توجد القريبة وما لم توجد القريبة لم توجد المعلول، وهو معنى الاحتياج وما ذكر من كون الشيء بماهيته علة لما هو علة لوجوده مع أنه محال ليس مما نحن فيه).
The sixth investigation: It is impossible for the occurrence of causality and effect to ascend infinitely, whether it is in finite subjects and is called circularity, or infinite, called infinite regress. As for the first: it is due to the impossibility of a thing being prior to itself in the sense that we say: (it existed so it existed, as is necessary in causality where it is correct to say: the movement of the hand existed, so the movement of the ring existed, unlike the reverse). For it is said: The priority of a thing to itself is not necessary, because that which needs a thing is not necessarily in need of that thing, since the proximate cause suffices, otherwise there would be delay; and because it is permissible for a thing by its essence to be a cause for what is a cause for its existence. We say: As long as the remote cause does not exist, the proximate cause does not exist, and as long as the proximate cause does not exist, the effect does not exist, and that is the meaning of need. And what is mentioned about a thing by its essence being a cause for what is a cause for its existence, while it is impossible, is not part of our subject.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the impossibility of causal regression ad infinitum. It begins by stating the general principle: it is impossible for causality and effect to ascend infinitely, either in finite subjects (circularity, دور) or infinite subjects (infinite regress, تسلسل). The first type, circularity, is impossible because it entails a thing being prior to itself in the sense of existence following from existence (the hand movement causing the ring movement, not vice versa). The author then presents an objection: priority to self is not necessary because the proximate cause may suffice, and a thing could be a cause for what is a cause for its existence in essence. The answer clarifies that without the remote cause, the proximate cause does not exist, and without that, the effect does not exist—this is genuine need. The objection's scenario is dismissed as impossible and irrelevant to the circularity discussed.

Key Terms
تراقي
عروض العلية والمعلولية

تسلسل
تقدم الشيء على نفسه
علة قريبة
علة بعيدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Circular causality could be possible if only the proximate cause is considered, since the proximate cause directly produces the effect without needing the remote cause.
Correction: The remote cause is necessary for the existence of the proximate cause; without the remote cause, the proximate cause would not exist, so the effect still depends on the entire chain. Circularity is impossible because it would make a thing rely on itself.

Checkpoint

B) It requires a thing to be prior to itself
C) The proximate cause is always sufficient
D) Causality must involve two distinct things

Key Takeaway
Circular causality is impossible because it requires a thing to be prior to itself, which is logically absurd.

The Impossibility of Circularity and Infinite Regress in Causality
Explanation of impossibility of circularity and infinite regress with proof and objection-response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why circular causality (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) are logically impossible, and how the argument relies on the concept of self-priority.

Core Idea
Circular causality forces a thing to be prior to itself, which is impossible.

In this passage, the author explains the impossibility of both circularity and infinite regress in causal chains. He introduces a comprehensive phrase encapsulating both, then provides a rigorous proof showing that circularity leads to self-priority—a logical impossibility. He also addresses a subtle objection about the type of priority involved.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense style and technical terms.
2
Identify the two problems
The passage defines two causal problems: circularity (dawr) when the chain of subjects is finite, and infinite regress (tasalsul) when it is infinite. Make sure you understand the distinction.
Circularity occurs when subjects of causality and effect repeat in a finite loop.
3
Follow the proof of impossibility
Trace the argument that circularity necessitates self-priority. The text uses transitivity: if A is cause of B, A is prior to B; if B is cause of A, B is prior to A; then A is prior to what is prior to A, so A is prior to itself.
Self-priority is deemed obviously impossible (badihi al-istihala).
4
Examine the objection and response
The objection suggests that 'priority' might be temporal, which is not necessary for causality. The response clarifies that the intended priority is existential, not temporal—the very sense that validates 'it existed so it existed'.
This shows that the impossibility is conceptual, not merely temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يريد بيان استحالة الدور والتسلسل ، وعبر عنهما بعبارة جامعة، وهي يتراقى عروض العلية والمعلولية لا إلى نهاية بأن يكون كل ما هو معروض للعلية، معروضاً للمعلولية، ولا ينتهي إلى ما تعرض له العلية دون المعلولية فإن كانت المعروضات متناهية فهو الدور بمرتبة إن كانا اثنتين وبمراتب إن كانت فوق الاثنتين، وإلا فهو التسلسل، أما بطلان الدور فلأنه يستلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه ، وهو ضروري الاستحالة . وجه الاستلزام أن الشيء إذا كان علة لآخر كان متقدماً عليه، وإذا كان الآخر علة له كان متقدماً عليه. والمتقدم على المتقدم على الشيء متقدم على ذلك الشيء، فيكون الشيء متقدماً على نفسه، ويلزمه كون الشيء متأخراً عن نفسه، وهو معنى احتياجه إلى نفسه، وتوقفه على نفسه، والكل بديهي الاستحالة، وربما يبين بأن التقدم أو التوقف أو الاحتياج نسبة لا تعقل إلا بين اثنين، وبأن نسبة المحتاج إليه إلى المحتاج الوجوب وعكسها وتوقف الإمكان والكل ضعيف . لأن التغاير الاعتباري كاف. فإن قيل : إن أريد بتقدم الشيء على نفسه التقدم بالزمان فغير لازم في العلة أو بالعلية، فنفس المدعى لأن قولنا لشيء لا يتقدم على نفسه بالعلية بمنزلة قولنا : الشيء لا يكون علة لنفسه .قلنا : المراد التقدم بالمعنى الذي يصحح قولنا وجد فوجد على ما هو.
He intends to explain the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress, and he expressed them with a comprehensive phrase, which is: the ascending occurrence of causality and effect infinitely, such that everything that is a subject of causality is a subject of effect, and it does not end with something that has causality without effect. If the subjects are finite, it is circularity: with one order if they are two, and with orders if more than two; otherwise it is infinite regress. As for the invalidity of circularity: because it necessitates the priority of a thing to itself, which is necessarily impossible. The manner of necessitation is that if a thing is a cause of another, it is prior to it, and if the other is a cause of it, it is prior to it. And what is prior to what is prior to a thing is prior to that thing, so the thing becomes prior to itself, and it follows that the thing is posterior to itself, which is the meaning of its need for itself and its dependence on itself—all of which is obviously impossible. And perhaps it is shown that priority, dependence, or need is a relation only conceivable between two things, and that the relation of the needed to the needy is necessity and its opposite, and the dependence of possibility—all of which is weak. Because conceptual distinction is sufficient. For it is said: If by the priority of a thing to itself is meant temporal priority, it is not necessary in the cause; or by causality, then that is the very claim, because our saying 'a thing is not prior to itself by causality' is equivalent to our saying 'a thing is not a cause of itself.' We say: What is meant is priority in the sense that validates our saying 'it existed so it existed' in the usual way.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress, with a detailed argument and response to an objection. The author begins by defining the comprehensive phrase: the ascending occurrence of causality and effect ad infinitum, where every subject of causality is also a subject of effect, and it does not end with a subject that has causality without effect. If the subjects are finite, it is circularity (whether in one order if two, or more orders if more than two); if infinite, it is infinite regress. The proof of the invalidity of circularity is that it necessitates self-priority, which is obviously impossible. The reasoning: if A is cause of B, A is prior to B; if B is cause of A, B is prior to A; and what is prior to what is prior to a thing is prior to that thing, so A is prior to itself, and also posterior to itself—this is need and dependence on self, all impossible. Other proofs (using relation between two, necessity and possibility) are weak because conceptual distinction suffices. An objection arises: if 'priority' means temporal priority, it is not necessary in causality; if causal priority, it is the very claim. The answer clarifies that the intended priority is the existential sense that validates 'it existed so it existed' in the usual way.

Definition of Circularity and Infinite Regress, and Proof of Circularity's Impossibility
Defines circularity and infinite regress, proves circularity impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of circularity and infinite regress in causal reasoning and the rational proof that circularity is impossible.

Core Idea
Circular causality forces a thing to be prior to itself, which is impossible.

This passage from the Islamic theological tradition introduces two critical logical problems in causal reasoning: circularity and infinite regress. The author first defines both in a unified expression, then provides a rigorous proof that circularity is impossible. Understanding this proof is essential for any argument about causal chains in theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Distinguish circularity from infinite regress
Notice the condition: if the series of causal attributions is finite, it is circularity; if infinite, it is infinite regress.
Circularity can have one level (two items) or multiple levels.
3
Trace the proof of circularity's impossibility
The proof shows that circularity leads to a thing being prior to itself, which is self-evidently impossible. Follow the logical steps: if A is cause of B, A prior to B; if B cause of A, B prior to A; then via transitivity, A prior to A.
This also implies dependence on oneself, which is absurd.
4
Reflect on the dismissal of alternative proofs
The author mentions alternative proofs based on relationality or necessity-contingency but dismisses them as weak because a mere conceptual distinction between cause and effect suffices.
Understand why the main proof is stronger.

He intends to explain the impossibility of circularity (al-dawr) and infinite regress (al-tasalsul), and he expressed them with a comprehensive expression: a succession of the attributions of causality and effecthood without end, in that everything that is an attribute of causality is an attribute of effecthood, and it does not end with something that has causality without effecthood. If the things that are attributes are finite, then it is circularity (al-dawr) with one level if they are two, and with multiple levels if they are more than two, otherwise it is infinite regress (al-tasalsul). As for the invalidity of circularity, it is because it entails the priority of the thing over itself, and that is necessarily impossible. The aspect of entailment is that if a thing is a cause of another, it is prior to it, and if the other is a cause of it, it is prior to it. That which is prior to that which is prior to a thing is prior to that thing, so the thing becomes prior to itself, and it entails the thing being posterior to itself, which is the meaning of its need for itself and its dependence on itself, and all of this is self-evidently impossible. And perhaps it is demonstrated by the fact that priority or dependence or need are relations that are only intelligible between two, and by the fact that the relation of the needed to the needy is necessity and its opposite is dependency of contingency, and all are weak, because conceptual distinction (al-taghayur al-i'tibari) is sufficient.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines two key concepts in causal reasoning: circularity (دور) and infinite regress (تسلسل), and then proves the impossibility of circularity. The author begins by stating that he intends to explain the impossibility of these two. He gives a comprehensive expression: 'a succession of the attributions of causality and effecthood without end,' meaning that everything that is a cause is also an effect, and it does not end with something that is only a cause and not an effect. He then specifies that if the series of such things is finite, it is circularity (with one level if there are only two items, or multiple levels if more than two); otherwise it is infinite regress. He then proves the invalidity of circularity: it entails the thing being prior to itself, which is necessarily impossible. The proof proceeds: if A is the cause of B, then A is prior to B; if B is the cause of A, then B is prior to A. Now whatever is prior to that which is prior to a thing is prior to that thing, so A is prior to itself. This also entails A being posterior to itself, which is the meaning of its need for itself and its dependence on itself. All these are self-evidently impossible. The author notes that some try to prove this by analyzing priority, dependence, and need as relations that require two distinct relata, or by considering the relation of necessary to contingent, but he dismisses these as weak, because a mere conceptual distinction (meaning the relational opposites like cause and effect are conceptually distinct even if the same thing is both) is sufficient to show the impossibility of self-dependence.

Objection on Precedence and the Answer
Sanusi refutes an objection on the meaning of precedence in causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between temporal and causal precedence and why the objection against the circularity proof fails.

Core Idea
Causal precedence means existential dependency, not temporal sequence.

This passage presents a subtle objection to the proof that circularity leads to self-precedence. The objector argues that if 'precedence' means temporal precedence, it is not necessary for a cause; if it means causal precedence, the argument is circular. The author clarifies that causal precedence is defined by the conditional 'if it existed, then it existed,' i.e., the effect depends on the cause. The hand-and-ring example illustrates that causal priority is about existential dependence, not time.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the dilemma: either temporal or causal precedence. Both are problematic for the proof.
The objector claims the argument is circular if causal precedence is meant.
2
Analyze the answer
The author defines causal precedence as the meaning that validates 'it existed so it existed'—existential dependence.
This is the same as saying the cause is that without which the effect would not exist.
3
Reflect on the example
Consider the hand and ring movement: the hand's movement is causally prior because the ring's movement depends on it. Self-precedence would require a thing to depend on itself, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إن أريد بتقدم الشيء على نفسه التقدم بالزمان فغير لازم في العلة أو بالعلية، فنفس المدعى لأن قولنا لشيء لا يتقدم على نفسه بالعلية بمنزلة قولنا : الشيء لا يكون علة لنفسه
قلنا : المراد التقدم بالمعنى الذي يصحح قولنا وجد فوجد على ما هو اللازم في كون الشيء علة للشيء، بمعنى أنه ما لم توجد العلة لم يوجد المعلول، ألا ترى أنه يصح أن يقال وجدت حركة اليد فوجدت حركة الخاتم ولا يصح أن يقال وجدت حركة الخاتم فوجدت حركة اليد، ولا خفاء في استحالة ذلك بالنظر إلى الشيء ونفسه .
For it is said: If by the precedence of a thing over itself is meant temporal precedence, then it is not necessary in the cause; or (if) causal precedence, then it is the very claim, because our saying 'a thing does not have causal precedence over itself' is equivalent to our saying 'a thing is not a cause of itself.' We said: The intended (precedence) is the meaning that validates our saying 'it existed so it existed' as is necessary in a thing being a cause of a thing, meaning that if the cause does not exist, the effect does not exist. Do you not see that it is valid to say: 'the movement of the hand existed, so the movement of the ring existed,' and it is not valid to say: 'the movement of the ring existed, so the movement of the hand existed'? And there is no obscurity in the impossibility of that with respect to the thing and itself.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the meaning of 'precedence' (تقدم) in the proof that circularity entails self-precedence. The objector raises a dilemma: If by 'precedence of a thing over itself' you mean temporal precedence, then that is not necessary for a cause (since cause and effect can be simultaneous). If you mean causal precedence, then the statement 'a thing does not have causal precedence over itself' is just another way of saying 'a thing is not the cause of itself,' which is exactly what you are trying to prove, making the argument circular. The author answers that the intended precedence is the meaning that validates the conditional statement 'it existed, so it existed' — that is, the cause is such that if it had not existed, the effect would not have existed. This is the necessary relationship in causality: the existence of the effect is conditional upon the prior existence of the cause. The author gives an example: It is valid to say 'the movement of the hand existed, so the movement of the ring existed' (because the hand's movement causes the ring's movement), but it is not valid to say the reverse, because the ring's movement does not cause the hand's movement. The impossibility of this relation holding between a thing and itself is clear: a thing cannot be such that its own existence depends on its own existence in the same way that the ring's movement depends on the hand's movement.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is valid because 'precedence' must be temporal or causal, and both are problematic.
Correction: The author clarifies that causal precedence is not about time but about existential dependency; the objection fails because it misunderstands the relevant sense.

Checkpoint

B) The movement of the hand causes the movement of the ring, so the hand's movement is prior in a causal sense.
C) The ring's movement causes the hand's movement.
D) The example shows that temporal precedence is the only valid sense of precedence.

Key Takeaway
Causal precedence is defined by the principle 'if the cause does not exist, the effect does not exist,' not by temporal priority.

Second Objection: Proximate Cause Sufficiency and Its Refutation
Objection that proximate cause suffices and answer proving remote cause necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that the proximate cause suffices, proving that the remote cause is necessary for the existence of the effect.

Core Idea
The remote cause is necessary because without it, the proximate cause itself cannot exist.

This passage continues the discussion on circular causality. An objector proposes that a proximate cause is sufficient for an effect, so a remote cause is not needed. The author answers that the proximate cause itself depends on the remote cause, so the effect ultimately depends on the remote cause. The objection's alternative scenarios are either impossible or irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its two grounds
Read the passage through once to grasp the objector's two arguments: first, that need is not transitive; second, that a thing could be the cause of its own cause by essence.
Notice the objector is trying to avoid circularity leading to self-precedence.
2
Identify the first ground
The first ground claims that if A needs B which needs C, A does not necessarily need C because B (the proximate cause) is sufficient for A.
This challenges the necessity of the remote cause.
3
Identify the second ground
The second ground suggests that a thing could, by its very essence, be the cause of something that is a cause of its existence, without requiring self-precedence.
This attempts to allow circularity without impossibility.
4
Analyze the answer
The author asserts the entailment is necessary. He refutes the first ground by showing that the remote cause is needed for the existence of the proximate cause, so the effect depends on it. He dismisses the second ground as both impossible (effect before cause) and irrelevant to the definition of circularity.
The key point: without the remote cause, the proximate cause does not exist.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that circularity entails self-precedence because the chain of dependence ultimately requires the remote cause. The objector's scenario of proximate cause sufficiency is false.
This reinforces the necessity of a first cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : يجوز أن يكون الشيء علة لما هو علة له من غير لزوم تقدم الشيء على نفسه وسند والمنع وجهان :
الأول : أن المحتاج إلى المحتاج إلى الشيء لا يلزم أن يكون محتاجاً إلى ذلك الشيء فإن العلة القريبة للشيء كافية في تحققه من غير احتياج إلى البعيدة، وإلا لزم تخلف الشيء عن علته القريبة .
والثاني : أن يكون الشيء بماهيته علة لشيء وهو علة لوجود ذلك الشيء .
قلنا اللزوم ضروري والسند مدفوع لأنه ما لم توجد العلة البعيدة للشيء لم توجد العلة القريبة، وما لم توجد العلة القريبة لم يوجد ذلك الشيء. فما لم توجد البعيدة لم يوجد ذلك الشيء وهو معنى الاحتياج والتخلف. إنما يلزم لو وجدت القريبة بدون البعيدة من غير وجود المعلول ولأن كون ماهية الشيء علة لما هو علة لوجوده، مع أنه ظاهر الاستحالة ، لما فيه من وجود المعلول قبل وجود العلة ليس مما نحن فيه . أعني الدور المفسر بتوقف الشيء على ما يتوقف عليه .
For it is said: It is permissible that a thing be a cause of that which is a cause of it without entailing the thing being prior to itself, and the proof (of necessity) is refuted in two ways: First, that which needs that which needs a thing is not necessarily in need of that thing, for the proximate cause of a thing is sufficient for its realization without need for the remote cause; otherwise the thing would be delayed from its proximate cause. Second, a thing by its essence could be a cause of something, and that something is a cause of the existence of that thing. We said: The entailment is necessary, and the refutation is rejected because if the remote cause of a thing does not exist, the proximate cause does not exist, and if the proximate cause does not exist, that thing does not exist. Therefore, if the remote cause does not exist, that thing does not exist, which is the meaning of need and delay. It (delay) would only be entailed if the proximate cause existed without the remote cause and the effect did not exist. And as for a thing's essence being a cause of that which is a cause of its existence, besides it being clearly impossible due to containing the existence of the effect before the existence of the cause, it is not what we are discussing, namely circularity defined as a thing depending on that which depends on it.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer, attempting to deny that circularity necessarily entails self-precedence. The objector proposes two grounds. First, that which needs that which needs a thing is not necessarily in need of that thing; for example, if A needs B and B needs C, A does not necessarily need C because B (the proximate cause) is sufficient for A, and requiring C would imply that A is delayed from B (i.e., that A would not exist even if B exists without C, which contradicts the sufficiency of the proximate cause). Second, a thing could by its very essence (ماهية) be the cause of something that is the cause of its existence, without requiring a separate self-precedence. The author answers decisively: the entailment of necessity (that circularity entails self-precedence) is necessary, and the refutation is rejected. The reason is that the existence of the proximate cause depends on the remote cause; if the remote cause does not exist, the proximate cause does not exist, and thus the final effect does not exist. Therefore, the final effect does depend on the remote cause, which is the meaning of need and delay. The objection's scenario of the proximate cause existing without the remote cause would only lead to the effect being delayed if the effect did not exist, but in the circular case, the remote cause is exactly the cause of the proximate cause. As for the second objection, that a thing's essence is the cause of what causes its own existence, besides being manifestly impossible (because it would require the effect to exist before its cause), it is not relevant to the definition of circularity under discussion, which is a thing depending on that which depends on it (توقف الشيء على ما يتوقف عليه).

Key Terms
علة قريبة
علة بعيدة
ماهية
احتياج
توقف
تخلف
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proximate cause alone is sufficient for the effect, so the remote cause is unnecessary.
Correction: The remote cause is necessary because the proximate cause itself depends on it; without the remote cause, the proximate cause does not exist.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why is the remote cause necessary for the final effect even if the proximate cause is sufficient?
A) Because the remote cause directly causes the effect.
B) Because without the remote cause, the proximate cause does not exist.
C) Because the remote cause is the true cause and the proximate cause is an illusion.
D) Because the objector admitted it.

Key Takeaway
The remote cause is necessary for the effect because the proximate cause itself cannot exist without it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you rely on 'proximate causes' like your income, health, or relationships. When was the last time you paused to acknowledge the 'remote cause' (Allah) without which these proximate causes wouldn't even exist? How would your gratitude and reliance change if you consciously traced every blessing back to Him?
?
The objector claimed that the proximate cause is sufficient, dismissing the need for the remote. Is there an area of your life where you feel self-sufficient, as if your own efforts are enough? What would it mean for you to truly admit that even your ability to act depends entirely on the Creator?
?
The author dismisses the idea of a thing being cause of its own cause as 'clearly impossible'. Yet we often fall into a subtle form of circularity: seeking causes within ourselves for our own existence or relying on created things as ultimate sources. Examine your heart: are you placing anything in the position of a 'remote cause' that only Allah deserves? How can you break that cycle today?

Objection and Response on Causal Precedence
Objection to causal precedence and two refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why causal precedence is necessary and how the objection of circularity is refuted.

Core Idea
Causal chains are not vicious circles; each cause must precede its effect.

This passage tackles a sophisticated objection to the necessity of causal precedence. The objector suggests that something could be both cause and effect without temporal priority. Sanusi responds by distinguishing proximate and remote causes and clarifying what a vicious circle truly means. Study this carefully to grasp a key distinction in causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening line: 'It is possible that a thing is a cause for that which is a cause for it without necessity of the thing preceding itself.' This is the challenge to the standard view.
The objector claims that mutual causation does not require temporal precedence.
2
Examine the first response
The author argues that the proximate cause suffices for the effect; need for the remote cause is not transitive. If A needs B which needs C, A does not necessarily need C.
This blocks the objection by denying that dependence transfers through the chain.
3
Examine the second response and conclusion
The second response points out that saying a thing's essence is cause for its own existence is a vicious circle, but this is not the same as a linear chain. The author affirms that precedence is necessary.
The objector confuses a circular dependency with a linear causal chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : يجوز أن يكون الشيء علة لما هو علة له من غير لزوم تقدم الشيء على نفسه وسند والمنع وجهان : الأول : أن المحتاج إلى المحتاج إلى الشيء لا يلزم أن يكون محتاجاً إلى ذلك الشيء فإن العلة القريبة للشيء كافية في تحققه من غير احتياج إلى البعيدة، وإلا لزم تخلف الشيء عن علته القريبة . والثاني : أن يكون الشيء بماهيته علة لشيء وهو علة لوجود ذلك الشيء . قلنا اللزوم ضروري والسند مدفوع لأنه ما لم توجد العلة البعيدة للشيء لم توجد العلة القريبة، وما لم توجد العلة القريبة لم يوجد ذلك الشيء. فما لم توجد البعيدة لم يوجد ذلك الشيء وهو معنى الاحتياج والتخلف. إنما يلزم لو وجدت القريبة بدون البعيدة من غير وجود المعلول ولأن كون ماهية الشيء علة لما هو علة لوجوده، مع أنه ظاهر الاستحالة، لما فيه من وجود المعلول قبل وجود العلة ليس مما نحن فيه . أعني الدور المفسر بتوقف الشيء على ما يتوقف عليه .
For it is said: It is possible that a thing is a cause for that which is a cause for it without necessity of the thing preceding itself. There is evidence [against that]. The prevention has two aspects: First: That the one in need of that which is in need of a thing does not necessarily need that thing. For the proximate cause of a thing is sufficient for its realization, without need for the remote cause. Otherwise, it would necessitate the thing being delayed from its proximate cause. Second: That the thing, by its essence, is a cause for something and it is a cause for the existence of that thing. We say: The necessity is necessary, and the evidence is repudiated because as long as the remote cause of the thing does not exist, the proximate cause does not exist, and as long as the proximate cause does not exist, that thing does not exist. So as long as the remote cause does not exist, that thing does not exist, and that is the meaning of need and delay. It only follows if the proximate cause existed without the remote cause without the existence of the effect. And because the thing's essence being a cause for what is a cause for its existence, despite it being clearly impossible, because of the existence of the effect before the existence of the cause, is not what we are discussing—I mean the vicious circle defined as the dependence of a thing on that which depends on it.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the author's two responses regarding the necessity of a cause's precedence over its effect. The objector argues that it is possible for something to be a cause of what is a cause of it without requiring that the cause precedes itself (i.e. without circular precedence). The author gives two responses. First response: Something that needs something else which itself needs a thing does not necessarily need that thing; the proximate cause is sufficient. Second response: The claim that a thing by its essence is a cause for something and that something is a cause for the thing's existence amounts to a vicious circle. The author asserts that the necessity of precedence is self-evident. The evidence for the objection is rejected because without the remote cause, the proximate cause does not exist, and without the proximate cause, the effect does not exist. The objection only holds if the proximate cause could exist without the remote cause, which is not the case. The passage ends by distinguishing this from the true vicious circle defined as the dependence of a thing on that which depends on it.

Key Terms
علة (cause)
معلول (effect)
علة قريبة (proximate cause)
علة بعيدة (remote cause)
دور (vicious circle)
محجاج (need)
تخلف (delay)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That dependence on a chain of causes implies a vicious circle.
Correction: A chain of causes where each depends on the prior is not circular because the dependence is linear, not reciprocal. A vicious circle requires each to depend on the other, which is not the case here.

Checkpoint
According to the first response in this passage, why is it not necessary for something that needs a thing to need that thing's own cause?
A) Because the proximate cause is sufficient.
B) Because the chain is infinite.
C) Because the effect does not need any cause.
D) Because the cause is self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Causal precedence is necessary; a chain of causes is not a vicious circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this lesson's emphasis on the sufficiency of the proximate cause (God's direct action) challenge that fear? Are you willing to trust that God is immediate and sufficient, without needing a long chain of intermediaries?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you see a chain of causes leading back to a first cause? Or do you subconsciously feel independent, as if you are your own cause? This lesson argues that every effect must have a preceding cause—will you today acknowledge that you are a creature in need of a Creator, and not self-sufficient?
?
If causal precedence is necessary and without the remote cause no proximate cause exists, what does that imply about your spiritual state? Are there 'remote causes' like repentance or purification that you are neglecting? Commit today to address that missing link so that the proximate cause of divine mercy can reach you.

Clarifying the Condition for the Cause of an Ordered Composite
Author clarifies the notion of a cause for a composite with ordered parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the independent cause of a composite with ordered parts must itself be ordered in its parts, and how this refutes arguments against infinite regress.

Core Idea
For an ordered composite, its cause must mirror that order.

This passage from al-Maqasid tackles a subtle point in causality: when something is composed of parts that are ordered in time or rank, its cause must share that order. The author uses this to defend the possibility of an infinite chain of causes, arguing that the need for a cause does not undermine the independence of the first cause. Let's read carefully to see how this reasoning unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: علة (cause), معلول (effect), مركب مرتّب الأجزاء (composite with ordered parts).
2
Identify the condition
The author states that the independent cause of an ordered composite must itself be ordered in its parts. Find this statement in the text.
3
Understand the 'pure effect'
The passage introduces an infinite chain where each part is a cause for the next, and the whole is a 'pure effect'. Reflect on how this relates to the condition.
4
See the refutation
The author uses this to refute the objection that there is no priority among parts. See how the argument invalidates that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرتب الأجزاء زماناً (۱) يلزم تقدم المعلول أو تخلفه عن المستقل بالإيجاد وإن أريد أنها تكون علة لكل جزء إما بنفسها (٢) أو لجزء منها بحيث لا يكون علة شيء من الأجزاء خارجة عن علة المركب، وتكون العلة المستقلة للمركب المرتب الأجزاء أيضاً مرتبة الأجزاء. وفي أجزاء السلسلة لا يمتنع أن يكون علة بهذا المعنى . كما قيل المعلول المحض لا إلى نهاية فإنه يقع لكل جزء منه جزء من السلسلة وهكذا كل مجموع ،قبله ، ولا يقدح في استقلاله (۳) بالإيجاد (٤) احتياجه في الوجود إلى علله أو احتياج السلسلة إلى المعلول المحض أيضاً، وبهذا يبطل الاستدلال بأنه لا أولوية لبعض الأجزاء، وبأن كل جزء يفرض فعليته أولى بالعلية (٥) هذا بعد تسليم احتياج السلسلة إلى غير علل الأجزاء، كيف ولا وجود لها غير وجودات الأجزاء؟ !
The ordered parts in time (1) necessitate the priority of the effect or its posteriority to the independent effector [creator]; and if it is intended that it is a cause for each part either by itself (2) or by a part of it, such that no cause of any of the parts is outside the cause of the composite, and the independent cause of the composite with ordered parts is itself ordered in its parts. And in the parts of the chain it is not impossible that it be a cause in this sense, as it was said: the pure effect ad infinitum, for each part of it falls a part of the chain, and likewise every aggregate preceding it; and its need in existence for its causes or the chain's need for the pure effect also does not impair its independence (3) in effecting (4). And by this the argument is invalidated that there is no priority for some parts, and that every part, if supposed actual, is more deserving of causality (5). This after conceding the chain's need for other than the causes of the parts. How, when there is no existence for it other than the existences of the parts?!
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature of a cause for a composite entity whose parts are ordered in time or rank. The author begins by stating that if one considers a cause for each part individually, the cause may either be the whole composite itself (by itself) or a part of it, but importantly the cause of any part should not be external to the cause of the whole. Moreover, the independent cause of an ordered composite must itself be ordered in its parts. Then the author introduces the concept of a 'pure effect' (المعلول المحض) in an infinite chain: each part of the chain is the cause of the next part in the same sense, and the whole chain is a pure effect with no external cause for its individual parts besides the chain itself. The author argues that the need of such a chain for its causes does not undermine the independence of its first part in effecting, because the chain as a whole is still dependent. This refutes an objection that there is no priority among parts in an infinite regress, or that every supposed actual part is more deserving of causality. The author notes that this refutation is valid only after accepting that the chain needs something beyond the causes of its parts, but questions: how can it have an existence other than the existences of its parts?

علة
معلول
مركب
مرتب الأجزاء
معلول محض
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite regress of causes is impossible because it would lack a priority or because each part is equally candidate for being cause.
Correction: The author shows that an infinite chain can be a pure effect where each part is caused by the next, and the condition of ordered parts is satisfied, thus invalidating the objection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is a condition for the independent cause of a composite with ordered parts?
A) It must be external to the composite.
B) It must be itself ordered in its parts.
C) It must be prior in time to all parts.
D) It must be a simple entity.

Key Takeaway
The independent cause of an ordered composite must itself be ordered in its parts, which refutes objections about priority in infinite regress.

?
The passage ends with a rhetorical question: 'How, when there is no existence for it other than the existences of the parts?' Reflect on the reality that the whole chain has no existence apart from its parts. In your own life, are you focusing on the parts (temporary causes) while neglecting the whole (the ultimate purpose and Cause)? What specific step can you take today to shift your focus?
?
This lesson argues that an infinite chain's need for causes does not impair the independence of its cause. Think of a situation where you felt overwhelmed by a chain of events or dependencies. How does this truth about an independent, ordered cause call you to trust in God's sovereignty and plan, even when you cannot see the full order?
