Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the objection successfully undermines the proof because equality and inequality only apply to finite quantities.
Correction: The author shows that equality can be understood as one-to-one correspondence, which applies to infinite sets, and that the objector's further claim about a 'cutoff' is false in an infinite series.

Checkpoint
What does the author first concede in response to the objection?
A) That equality is impossible between infinite sets.
B) That equality can mean one-to-one correspondence, which is possible for infinite sets.
C) That the disjunctive proposition is invalid.
D) That the series is finite.

Key Takeaway
The objection based on the finite nature of equality is refuted by redefining equality as one-to-one correspondence, which holds even for infinite sets, and by denying that any part of an infinite series is finite.

Refuting Equality/Inequality in Infinite Series
The author denies the disjunctive proposition about equality or inequality between two infinite series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the disjunctive proposition claiming equality or inequality between two infinite series is invalid, and how infinite series can be compared through one-to-one correspondence without contradiction.

Core Idea
Equality and inequality apply only to finite quantities.

This lesson examines a key objection against arguments involving infinite series. The author refutes the claim that two infinite series must be either equal or unequal in the finite sense, and instead shows how one-to-one correspondence can be used without contradiction, preserving consistency in discussions of infinity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive proposition
Read the passage and find the disjunctive proposition that the author is refuting: that one infinite series is equal to, greater than, or less than another.
The proposition is stated at the beginning: 'this is equal to that or more or less.'
2
Understand why equality is a property of the finite
The author states that equality and difference (inequality) are properties of the finite. Reflect on why this is a crucial principle in dealing with infinities.
Finite quantities can be compared by magnitude; infinite quantities cannot.
3
Learn the alternative definition of equality
The author introduces a one-to-one correspondence definition: 'opposite every part of this there is a part of that.' Notice that this does not involve magnitude and is possible for infinite sets.
Example: one-to-one between numbers 1,2,3... and 10,20,30...
4
See why the smaller series cannot be finite
The objector claims the smaller series must be finite, but the author argues that if the series is infinite, any part from the infinite side is also infinite.
Thus, the 'smaller' infinite series is still infinite, not cut off.
5
Recognize the refutation as complete
The passage ends by calling the objection 'false' (كاذب). Understand that the argument successfully defends the consistency of infinite series comparisons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما سبق منع المنفصلة القائلة (١) بأن هذا مساو لذاك أو أكثر أو أقل، فإن التساوى والتفاوت من خواص المتناهي وإن أريد بالتساوى مجرد أن يقع بإزاء كل جزء من هذا جزء من ذاك، فلا نسلم استحالته فيما بين العدتين كما في الواحد إلى مالا يتناهى والعشرة إلى ما لا يتناهى وكون أحدهما أضعاف (٢) الآخر لا ينافي التساوى بهذا المعنى ولو سلم. فمنع كون الأقل منقطعاً، فإن السلسلة إذا كانت غير متناهية كان بعضها الذى من الجانب الغير المتناهي أيضاً غير متناه وكذا عدة ألوفها أو مئاتها أو عشراتها، وحديث الجملتين، وانقطاع أولاهما بمبدأ الثانية كاذب .
What preceded prevented the disjunctive proposition saying that this is equal to that or more or less. For equality and difference are properties of the finite. If by equality is meant merely that opposite every part of this there is a part of that, then we do not admit its impossibility between two sets, as in the one to infinity and the ten to infinity. And the fact that one is a multiple of the other does not negate equality in this sense. And if it is granted, then he prevents the smaller from being finite. For if the series is infinite, then a part of it from the infinite side is also infinite, and likewise the number of its thousands, hundreds, or tens. And the talk of the two sequences, and the first being cut off at the beginning of the second, is false.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection concerning the comparison of two infinite series. The author begins by stating that the preceding discussion already prevented the disjunctive proposition that one infinite series is equal to, greater than, or less than another. He explains that equality and difference (inequality) are properties that belong only to finite things. If one redefines equality as a one-to-one correspondence (that opposite every part of one there is a part of the other), then such equality is not impossible between two infinite sets. He gives the example of the series from one to infinity and from ten to infinity: even if one is a multiple of the other, this does not violate equality in this sense. Even if this redefinition is granted, the objector still claims that the smaller series must be finite (cut off). The author denies this, arguing that if the series is infinite, then any part of it taken from the infinite side is also infinite. This applies to the number of thousands, hundreds, or tens as well. Thus, the claim that one series is cut off at the beginning of the other is false.

المنفصلة (disjunctive proposition)
التساوى (equality)
التفاوت (difference/inequality)
المتناهي (finite)
غير المتناهي (infinite)
السلسلة (series)
العدتين (two sets)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that equality and inequality apply to infinite sets as they do to finite ones.
Correction: The author shows that equality and inequality are properties of the finite; for infinite sets, one-to-one correspondence can be used without implying magnitude comparison.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the disjunctive proposition about equality or inequality between two infinite series prevented?
A) Because infinite series are never equal or unequal.
B) Because equality and inequality are properties of the finite.
C) Because the smaller series must always be finite.
D) Because one-to-one correspondence is impossible between infinite series.

Key Takeaway
Infinite series can be compared through one-to-one correspondence without falling into the false dichotomy of equality or inequality in the finite sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often compare your deeds to others, feeling superior or inferior. But this lesson says equality and inequality are only for finite things. How does the infinite nature of Allah’s mercy and your potential for growth change how you view your own shortcomings? What specific comparison will you stop making today?
?
If you believe Allah’s attributes are infinite, why do you sometimes act as if His mercy is limited by your sins? The lesson shows that even a multiple cannot exhaust an infinite series. Will you repent of one sin today, trusting that His mercy is truly infinite and not diminished by your failures?
?
Do you treat your blessings as finite commodities to be hoarded? The one-to-one correspondence between infinite sets suggests that even if others seem to have more, you are not lacking in the sight of Allah. What blessing will you count as truly infinite today, and how will you share it with someone else?

Matter as Substrate and the Rule of Two Forms
Definition of matter as substrate and the rule about two forms in one or two degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that matter is defined as the substrate, receptacle, and bearer for form, and that matter cannot be constituted by two forms in the same degree of composition, but can have forms in different degrees.

Core Idea
Matter is the receptive foundation; form is the shaping principle.

This lesson introduces the foundational definitions of matter and form. The author establishes that matter serves as the passive substrate and receptacle, while form is linked to efficient causation through an agent or part of an agent. A key rule is also presented: matter cannot have two forms in the same compositional degree, whether independent or combined, but it can have forms in different degrees, such as the corporeal form and the specific form.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning through the example of corporeal and specific forms. Focus on the three roles of matter: substrate, receptacle, bearer.
The phrase 'محل وقابل وحامل' is a standard description of matter.
2
Understand form's relation to agency
Note that form is said to have an agent or part of an agent. This means form participates in efficient causation, but does not act independently.
This sets up the causal explanation in the next lesson.
3
Examine the rule about two forms
The author states that matter cannot be constituted by two forms in one degree. Consider why that would make the composite not one whole. Then see the exception: two forms in different degrees are allowed, giving the example of the corporeal form and the specific form.
Think of the corporeal form as the general shape and the specific form as the unique essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السابع المادة للصورة محل وقابل وحامل (قال : المادة للصورة محل وقابل وحامل، والصورة لها فاعل أو جزء فاعل ولا تتقوم المادة بصورتين في درجة إما استقلالاً فظاهر وإما اجتماعاً، فلأن القوم حينئذ هو المجموع ، وهو واحد، ويجوز في درجتين كالصورة الجسمية والنوعية . لما كانت الجزئية معتبرة في مفهومي المادة والصورة لم يكونا مادة وصورة إلا باعتبار الإضافة إلى المركب منهما وإما باعتبار إضافة كل منهما إلى الأخرى، فالمادة محل وقابل وحامل للصورة والصورة جزء فاعل لها بمعنى أن فيضان وجود المادة عن الفاعل يكون بإعانة من الصورة ضرورة احتياج المادة إليها مع امتناع استقلالها بالعلية لأن المادة إنما تحتاج إلى الصورة من حيث هي صورة، وإلا من حيث هي تلك الصورة المعينة ضرورة بقائها عند انعدام الصورة المعينة والصورة من حيث هي صورة مالا تكون واحدة بالعدد فلا يمكن أن تكون علة مستقلة للمادة الواحدة بالعدد، وإنما لم يجعلوا المادة جزء فاعل للصورة بناء على احتياج الصورة إليها لما تقرر عندهم من أن شأن المادة القبول لا الفعل .
The seventh investigation: matter as substrate, receptacle, and bearer for form. He said: matter is substrate, receptacle, and bearer for form, and form has an agent or part of an agent. Matter is not constituted by two forms in one degree – either independently, that is apparent, or in combination, for then the composite is the whole, and it is one. And it is permissible in two degrees like the corporeal form and the specific form. Since partiality is considered in the concepts of matter and form, they are not matter and form except by virtue of the relation to the composite of the two or by virtue of the relation of each to the other. So matter is substrate, receptacle, and bearer for form, and form is a part of its agent in the sense that the emanation of the existence of matter from the agent occurs with the assistance of form due to the necessity of matter's need for it, along with the impossibility of its being independent in causality, because matter only needs form insofar as it is form, not insofar as it is that specific form, because of its [matter's] remaining when that specific form ceases to exist. And form insofar as it is form is not one in number, so it cannot be an independent cause for a matter that is one in number. And they did not make matter a part of the agent for form based on form's need for it, because it is established with them that the function of matter is reception, not action.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between matter and form. It begins by stating that matter is the substrate, receptacle, and bearer for form. Form has an agent or is part of an agent (meaning it contributes to efficient causation). The author explains that matter cannot be constituted by two forms in the same degree, either independently (obviously) or together, because then the composite would be one whole. However, it can have forms in different degrees, like the corporeal form (الصورة الجسمية) and the specific form (الصورة النوعية). He then notes that the concepts of matter and form involve partiality: they are only considered matter and form relative to the composite or to each other. Thus, matter is the substrate for form, and form is a part of the efficient cause of matter: the agent's emanation of matter's existence is assisted by the form, because matter needs form. Matter needs form as form, not as a specific form, because matter remains when the specific form passes away. Form as form is not numerically one, so it cannot be the independent cause of numerically one matter. Finally, matter is not made a part of the efficient cause of form because form also needs matter, but matter's role is reception (القبول), not action (الفعل).

فاعل (agent)
جزء فاعل (part of an agent)

Key Takeaway
Matter is the receptive substrate; form is associated with agency, and matter cannot have two forms in the same degree of composition.

The passage says form has an agent or part of an agent. When you look at your own existence, do you see yourself as mere matter shaped by circumstance, or do you recognize the Divine Agent who gave you your specific form? How will you live today as a conscious bearer of that form?

Form as Partial Efficient Cause of Matter
Explanation of form as partial efficient cause and matter's receptive role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how form acts as a partial efficient cause for matter, why matter needs form as form (not a specific form), why form cannot be an independent cause, and why matter's role is reception, not action.

Core Idea
Form assists in causing matter, but matter's sole function is to receive.

This lesson delves into the causal relationship between matter and form. The author explains that form is a part of the efficient cause of matter: the emanation of matter's existence from the agent is aided by form because matter needs form. However, this need is for form as such, not for any particular specific form, since matter persists even when a specific form is lost. Moreover, form as such is not numerically one, so it cannot independently cause a numerically one matter. Finally, the author clarifies why matter is not made a part of the efficient cause of form: matter's role is to receive, not to act.

Guided Path
1
Read the causal argument
Read the second part of the passage starting from 'لما كانت الجزئية معتبرة'. Focus on the reasoning about form's role in efficient causation.
This part builds on the definitions from the first lesson.
2
Understand form as part of the efficient cause
The author says that the emanation (فيضان) of matter's existence from the agent occurs with the assistance (بإعانة) of form. This means form contributes to the causation of matter.
Form is not the full cause, but a partial efficient cause.
3
Distinguish need for form as such vs. specific form
Matter needs form in general, not a particular form, because matter remains when a specific form (like the form of fire) ceases to exist. If it needed the specific form, it would cease to exist when that form disappears.
This shows matter's ability to receive different forms.
4
Understand why form cannot be an independent cause
Form as form is not numerically one; it is a universal concept. Therefore it cannot be the cause of a numerically one (particular) matter. The cause must be particular.
This is a logical argument based on the one-to-one correspondence of cause and effect.
5
Learn why matter is not an agent for form
Although form needs matter as a substrate, the philosophers do not call matter a part of the agent for form because matter's essential function is reception (القبول), not action (الفعل).
This reinforces the asymmetry between matter and form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Final Cause: A Special Kind of Cause
Explains that the end (ghaya) is a cause in a specific way.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the final cause (ghaya) is a cause in a qualified sense: it is a cause in mental existence and for the agent's agency, but an effect in external existence.

Core Idea
The final cause exists mentally to motivate, not externally to produce.

In this passage, the author introduces a new topic: the final cause (ghaya). He clarifies that the end is a cause for the thing only in a specific way—it is not a cause like the agent. Rather, the agent needs to conceive the end in order to act, so the end is a cause for the agent's agency. But in external existence, the end is actually an effect of the agent's act. This distinction between mental and external existence is crucial.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage in Arabic and follow the literal translation. Notice how the author distinguishes between two aspects of the end: quiddity and constitution.
The text ends abruptly at 'external existence'. The meaning is clear from context: in external existence, the end is an effect.
2
Understand the key distinction
The passage says the end is a cause for the agent's agency in its quiddity (bi-mahiyatiha), but in its constitution (bi-baniha) it is an effect. This means: considered as a concept, the end motivates; considered as an existing thing, it results from action.
3
Relate to mental vs. external existence
The author states the end is a cause in mental existence and (implicitly) an effect in external existence. This clarifies how the end functions in causation.
Think of a builder conceiving the house (end) before building; the house-as-concept motivates, but the actual house is built.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وأما غاية الشيء فإنما تكون علة له من حيث احتياجه إلى علته المفتقرة علتها إلى تصور الغاية، ولهذا قالوا إنها بماهيتها علة لفاعلية الفاعل وبانيتها معلول له بل لمعلوله، وأنها بالوجود الذهني علة، وبالوجود العيني
He said: As for the end (ghaya) of a thing, it is only a cause for it insofar as it [the thing] needs its cause, whose cause is in need of the conception of the end. For this reason they said that it [the end] is, in its quiddity (bi-mahiyatiha), a cause for the agency of the agent, and in its constitution (bi-baniha) it is an effect of it, rather of its effect. And that it is, in mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni), a cause, and in external existence (al-wujud al-'ayni)...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage begins a new topic on the final cause (ghaya). It states that the end is a cause for the thing (the effect) because the thing's cause (the agent) needs to conceive the end. This implies that the end is a cause in a specific sense: in its quiddity (what it is), it is a cause for the agent's act (i.e., the agent acts for the sake of the end). However, in its external existence, the end is an effect of the agent's act, not a cause. The author then notes that the end is a cause in mental existence (as a concept) and in external existence it is ... (the sentence is cut off). The key distinction is between the end as a mental concept motivating the agent and the end as an actual existent that results from the act.

Key Terms
غاية
علة
فاعل
ماهية
وجود ذهني
وجود عيني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the end is a cause in the same way the agent is a cause (i.e., as an efficient cause that produces an effect).
Correction: The passage clarifies that the end is a cause only in mental existence and as a cause for the agent's agency, not as an external efficient cause. In external existence, the end is an effect.

Checkpoint

A) In both mental and external existence equally.
B) In mental existence as a cause, but in external existence it is an effect.
C) Only in external existence.
D) Only as a formal cause.

Key Takeaway
The final cause (ghaya) is a cause in mental existence and for the agent's agency, but an effect in external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The end in external existence is an effect, not a cause. How does this truth challenge your pride? Can you accept that the final result you achieve is not the cause of your effort but its consequence – and that the true cause lies in your conception of it?

The Final Cause as a Cause in Mental Existence
Al-Taftazani explains how the final cause is a cause in mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the final cause can be a cause for the agent's agency despite being posterior, and the distinction between its causal role in mental existence and its effect in external existence.

Core Idea
The final cause is a cause in the mind, not in external reality.

Al-Taftazani addresses a puzzle: how can the final cause (the goal) be a cause when it comes after the act? He explains that the final cause is a cause only in mental existence—it is the conceived end that motivates the agent. This lesson unpacks this key distinction and the relationship between the final cause, the agent's agency, and the effect.

Guided Path
1
Understand the puzzle
The final cause is temporally posterior to the act, yet it is called a cause. This seems paradoxical.
Consider why something later can be considered a cause.
2
Grasp the solution
The final cause is a cause in mental existence (the conceived end) for the agent's agency, not in external existence.
The distinction between mental and external existence is crucial.
3
Examine the example
The carpenter conceives sitting on the bed, which causes him to make the bed, then sitting occurs. The conceived sitting is the final cause.
4
Learn the principle
The saying 'the first of thought is the last of action' encapsulates that the end is first in conception but last in execution.
This summarizes the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما غاية الشيء فإنما تكون علة له من حيث احتياجه إلى علته المفتقرة علتها إلى تصور الغاية، ولهذا قالوا إنها بماهيتها علة لفاعلية الفاعل وبانيتها معلول له بل لمعلوله، وأنها بالوجود الذهني علة، وبالوجود العيني معلول. نعم: قد تطلق الغاية على ما ينتهي إليه الفعل وإن لم يكن مقصوداً، وإن لم يكن للفاعل قصد واختيار، وبهذا الاعتبار أثبتوا للطبيعيات والاتفاقيات غايات، وجعلوا من الغاية اتفاقية وهي ما لا يكون تأدي السبب إليه دائماً ولا أكثرياً.
يريد بيان علته الغائية دفعاً لما يستبعد من كون المتأخر عن الشيء علة له. بمعنى كون غاية الشيء علة له إن ذلك الشيء يفتقر في وجوده العيني إلى وجودها العقلي بواسطة أنه يحتاج إلى عليته الفاعلية، وهي في كونها علة تحتاج إلى تصور الغائية، ضرورة أن الفاعل ما لم يتصور الغاية ما لا يفعل إلا لغاية لم يفعله. ومن هاهنا قالوا: إن الغاية بماهيتها أي بصورتها الذهنية علة لفاعلية الفاعل، وبانيتها أي هويتها الخارجية معلول للفاعل، بل المعلول الذي هو مآل الغاية، فإن النجارية صور الجلوس على السرير فيوجده، ثم يوجد الجلوس عليه. وللقوم عبارة أخرى وهو أن الغاية بالوجود الذهني علة، وبالوجود العيني معلول، وهذا معنى قولهم: أول الفكر آخر العمل.
فإن قيل: الغاية قد لا تكون معلولاً بل قديماً كما يقال: الواجب تعالى غاية الغايات، وقد لا يكون مقصوداً للفاعل وإن كان مختاراً كالعثور على الكنز في حفر البئر، وقد لا يكون للفاعل قصد واختيار كغاية الحركات الغير الإرادية، مثل الوصول إلى الأرض كهبوط الحجر. قلنا: قد تطلق الغاية على ما ينتهي إليه الفعل، وإن لم يكن مقصوداً
As for the final cause of a thing, it is a cause for it only insofar as it (the thing) needs its cause, and its cause is in need of the conception of the final cause. Therefore they said: it is, by its quiddity, a cause for the agent's agency, and by its identity (bāniyyatihā) an effect for it—rather for its effect—and that it is a cause in mental existence and an effect in external existence. Yes: the term 'final cause' may be applied to that at which the act terminates, even if it is not intended, and even if the agent has no purpose or choice; in this sense they posited final causes for natural and accidental things, and they made part of final causes 'accidental', which is what the leading of the cause to it is neither perpetual nor predominant.
He intends to explain the final cause, repelling what is deemed improbable, namely that what is posterior to a thing is a cause for it. That is, the fact that the final cause of a thing is a cause for it means that that thing depends in its external existence upon its mental existence, by means of its need for its agentive cause, which in being a cause needs the conception of the finality, necessarily because the agent—unless it conceives the final cause, and the final cause is not done except for a purpose—does not do it. Hence they said: the final cause is, by its quiddity—i.e., its mental form—a cause for the agent's agency, and by its identity—i.e., its external quiddity—an effect of the agent, rather the effect which is the outcome of the final cause. For the carpenter conceives sitting on the bed and thus produces it, then sitting on it occurs. And for the people there is another expression: that the final cause is a cause in mental existence and an effect in external existence, and this is the meaning of their saying: 'the first of thought is the last of action.'
If it is said: the final cause may not be an effect but rather eternal, as it is said the Necessary Exalted is the final cause of final causes; and it may not be intended by the agent even if he is choiceful, like stumbling upon a treasure in digging a well; and there may be no purpose and choice for the agent, like the final cause of non-voluntary movements, such as reaching the ground in the falling of a stone. We say: the term 'final cause' may be applied to that at which the act terminates, even if it is not intended.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of the final cause (al-ghāya) and how it can be a cause despite being temporally or ontologically posterior. The author begins by stating that the final cause of a thing is a cause only insofar as that thing needs its cause, and that cause (the agent's causality) requires conception of the final cause. Hence, the final cause is, in terms of its quiddity (māhiyya), a cause for the agent's agency (fā'iliyya), but in terms of its external identity (bāniyya), it is an effect of the agent—indeed, an effect of the effect (the effect's effect). In other words, it is a cause in mental existence (wujūdhihnī) and an effect in external existence (wujūd 'aynī). The author then clarifies that the term 'final cause' can be used more broadly for what the act terminates at, even if not intended, as in natural and accidental phenomena. Then the author explains that this is meant to remove the paradox of the posterior being a cause: the thing depends on the mental existence of its final cause because it needs the agent's causality, which requires the conception of the end. An example: the carpenter conceives sitting on the bed, which causes him to produce the bed, and then the sitting occurs. This is expressed as 'the first of thought is the last of action.' Three objections are raised: (1) the final cause may be eternal (like God), (2) it may not be intended (like treasure in digging), (3) it may be non-voluntary (like stone falling). The answer: the term 'final cause' can be applied to what the act terminates at, even if not intended, thus covering these cases.

وجود ذهني
وجود عيني

ماهية
هوية خارجية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the final cause is a cause in external reality because it is the actual end achieved.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the final cause is a cause only in mental existence (the conceived end), not in external existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in what sense is the final cause a cause for the agent's agency?
A) In external existence, because it is the actual end attained.
B) In mental existence, because the agent must conceive it to act.
C) In both mental and external existence equally.
D) Only if it is intended and voluntary.

Key Takeaway
The final cause is a cause for the agent's agency in the mind, not in external reality; the conceived end precedes the act in thought, not in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in the Day of Judgment as a final cause for your actions. Does that conception actually move your agency? Or is it merely a mental form without effect? What would change in your daily life if you truly let that final cause cause your agency?
?
The carpenter conceives sitting on the bed and thus produces the bed. What final causes are you conceiving each day? Are they noble or base? Examine your first thought before each action—that conception is the real cause of your deed. How can you train yourself to conceive Allah's pleasure first?
?
The final cause is 'first in thought, last in action.' But your actions often follow desires without prior thought. Are you acting without a final cause? What does that say about your agency? Commit to pausing before each action to consciously conceive a purpose that pleases Allah.

Broadening the Definition of Final Cause: Objections and Answer
Al-Taftazani responds to three objections against the final cause and broadens its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'final cause' is used more broadly to include ends that are not intended, not voluntary, or eternal, and how the objection that the final cause cannot be a cause in such cases is answered.

Core Idea
The final cause can be any termination point of an act, not just an intended goal.

Having established that the final cause is a cause in mental existence, Al-Taftazani addresses three objections: the final cause might be eternal (like God), unintended (like treasure in digging), or involuntary (like a stone falling). He responds by noting that the term 'final cause' can be applied to any end point, whether intended or not, thereby resolving the apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
The objections: (1) final cause may be eternal (not an effect), (2) may be unintended (e.g., treasure), (3) may be involuntary (e.g., stone falling).
Each objection seems to violate the condition of being a cause in mental existence.
2
Understand the shared problem
Each case appears to lack an intended purpose or the effect relationship, challenging the earlier definition.
3
See the answer
Al-Taftazani answers by broadening the definition: 'final cause' can apply to any termination point of an act, regardless of intention or voluntariness.
This answer neatly resolves all three objections.
4
Apply the concept
This broader definition allows final causes in natural and accidental phenomena, as mentioned earlier.

وأما غاية الشيء فإنما تكون علة له من حيث احتياجه إلى علته المفتقرة علتها إلى تصور الغاية، ولهذا قالوا إنها بماهيتها علة لفاعلية الفاعل وبانيتها معلول له بل لمعلوله، وأنها بالوجود الذهني علة، وبالوجود العيني معلول. نعم: قد تطلق الغاية على ما ينتهي إليه الفعل وإن لم يكن مقصوداً، وإن لم يكن للفاعل قصد واختيار، وبهذا الاعتبار أثبتوا للطبيعيات والاتفاقيات غايات، وجعلوا من الغاية اتفاقية وهي ما لا يكون تأدي السبب إليه دائماً ولا أكثرياً.
يريد بيان علته الغائية دفعاً لما يستبعد من كون المتأخر عن الشيء علة له. بمعنى كون غاية الشيء علة له إن ذلك الشيء يفتقر في وجوده العيني إلى وجودها العقلي بواسطة أنه يحتاج إلى عليته الفاعلية، وهي في كونها علة تحتاج إلى تصور الغائية، ضرورة أن الفاعل ما لم يتصور الغاية ما لا يفعل إلا لغاية لم يفعله. ومن هاهنا قالوا: إن الغاية بماهيتها أي بصورتها الذهنية علة لفاعلية الفاعل، وبانيتها أي هويتها الخارجية معلول للفاعل، بل المعلول الذي هو مآل الغاية، فإن النجارية صور الجلوس على السرير فيوجده، ثم يوجد الجلوس عليه. وللقوم عبارة أخرى وهو أن الغاية بالوجود الذهني علة، وبالوجود العيني معلول، وهذا معنى قولهم: أول الفكر آخر العمل.

As for the final cause of a thing, it is a cause for it only insofar as it (the thing) needs its cause, and its cause is in need of the conception of the final cause. Therefore they said: it is, by its quiddity, a cause for the agent's agency, and by its identity (bāniyyatihā) an effect for it—rather for its effect—and that it is a cause in mental existence and an effect in external existence. Yes: the term 'final cause' may be applied to that at which the act terminates, even if it is not intended, and even if the agent has no purpose or choice; in this sense they posited final causes for natural and accidental things, and they made part of final causes 'accidental', which is what the leading of the cause to it is neither perpetual nor predominant.
He intends to explain the final cause, repelling what is deemed improbable, namely that what is posterior to a thing is a cause for it. That is, the fact that the final cause of a thing is a cause for it means that that thing depends in its external existence upon its mental existence, by means of its need for its agentive cause, which in being a cause needs the conception of the finality, necessarily because the agent—unless it conceives the final cause, and the final cause is not done except for a purpose—does not do it. The Broad and Accidental Meaning of Ghaya (End)
Defining the broad sense of ghaya and introducing the accidental end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'ghaya' (end/purpose) can be used in a broad sense to include ends that are not intended nor regularly caused, and how the accidental ghaya is defined by irregular causation.

Core Idea
Ghaya can refer to any endpoint of reasoning, even without intention or regular causation.

This passage expands the concept of ghaya beyond the usual association with intentional causation. The author explains that the term can apply to any conclusion reached by the mind, even for natural or accidental events. This sets the stage for a nuanced understanding of final causality in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key terms ghaya and ghaya ittifaqiyya.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two main claims
Find where the author says ghaya can be used for any mental endpoint (not just effect/intention) and where accidental ghaya is defined by irregular causation.
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this broad definition matters in kalam: it allows discussing ends in nature and chance without implying intention.
This connects to discussions of causality in natural philosophy.
4
Test your understanding

The author states that 'ghaya' can be used for the endpoint of reasoning, regardless of whether it is an actual effect or intended by an agent. This allows for ends in natural processes and accidental occurrences. The passage introduces the distinction between essential (dhatiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya) ends, defined by regularity of causation.

The Final Cause as Cause of the Efficient Cause
Al-Sanusi explains why the final cause is a cause despite being posterior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the final cause, though posterior in external existence, is a cause of the efficient cause through its mental existence.

Core Idea
The end in the mind is the cause of the cause.

This passage tackles a subtle point in causal reasoning: how can something that comes after (the final end) be a cause? The author, al-Sanusi, resolves this by distinguishing between mental and external existence. He argues that the final cause is a cause in the mind, even though it is an effect in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice how the author presents a problem and its solution.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key distinction
Focus on the distinction between mental existence (wujud dhihni) and external existence (wujud 'aini). The final cause is a cause in the former and an effect in the latter.
3
Understand the carpenter example
The example of the carpenter who forms a mental image of sitting before building the chair illustrates the point. Explain how the mental image (ghaya) causes the carpenter to act.
This example makes the abstract reasoning concrete.
4
Grasp the maxim
The passage ends with a famous maxim: 'The beginning of thought is the end of action.' Consider how this summarizes the causal relationship.
Memorize this maxim as it captures the essence.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to check your comprehension of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يريد بيان علته الغائية دفعاً لما يستبعد من كون المتأخر عن الشيء علة له. بمعنى كون غاية الشيء علة له إن ذلك الشيء يفتقر في وجوده العيني إلى وجودها العقلى بواسطة أنه يحتاج إلى عليته الفاعلية وهى في كونها علة تحتاج إلى تصور الغائية ضرورة أن الفاعل ما لم يتصور، وغاية ما لا يفعل إلا لغاية لم يفعله ومن هاهنا قالوا: إن الغاية بماهيتها أي بصورتها الذهنية علة الفاعلية الفاعل وبانيتها أى هويتها الخارجية معلول للفاعل، بل المعلوله الذى هو مآله الغاية، فإن النجارية صور الجلوس على السرير فيوجده، ثم يوجد الجلوس عليه وللقوم عبارة أخرى وهو أن الغاية بالوجود الذهني علة، وبالوجود العينى معلول وهذا معنى قولهم: أول الفكر آخر العمل.
He intends to clarify its final cause (ghaya) in order to repel what seems improbable: that which is posterior to a thing is a cause of it. Meaning that the ghaya of a thing is a cause of it, in that that thing depends in its external existence upon the mental existence of its ghaya, by way of its need for an efficient cause, and the efficient cause, in being a cause, needs to conceive the final cause necessarily, because the agent, as long as he has not conceived of the end, and the end is not aimed at, he does not act. Hence they said: the ghaya, in its quiddity i.e., its mental form, is the cause of the agent's being an efficient cause, and in its entity i.e., its external identity, it is an effect of the agent, nay, the effect which is the outcome of the ghaya. For the carpenter forms a mental image of sitting on the chair, and then he produces it, then sitting on it occurs. And they have another expression: the ghaya in mental existence is a cause, and in external existence it is an effect. This is the meaning of their saying: the beginning of thought is the end of action.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents the proof that the final cause (ghaya) is indeed a cause, despite being posterior in external existence. The author argues that a thing's existence depends on its cause: the efficient cause acts only after conceiving an end. Thus the mental conception of the end is a cause of the efficient cause's causal efficacy. The example of the carpenter who mental images sitting (the end) before building the chair illustrates this. The passage concludes that the end in the mind is a cause, in reality an effect — captured by the maxim 'the beginning of thought is the end of action.'

Key Terms

علة فاعلية
وجود ذهني
وجود عيني
صورة ذهنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the final cause is causally posterior in every sense, so it cannot be a cause at all.
Correction: The final cause is prior in mental existence, which is sufficient to be a cause of the efficient cause's action.

Checkpoint
Why does the efficient cause need to conceive the final cause before acting?
A) Because the final cause provides the material for the act.
B) Because the agent must know what he is aiming at to act deliberately.
C) Because the final cause is the formal cause.
D) Because the final cause is the same as the efficient cause.

Key Takeaway
The final cause is a cause in mental existence, not in external existence; it gives the efficient cause its reason to act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Objection and Answer: Ghaya as Mere Termination
Objection and answer: three exceptions to ghaya's definition and the response redefining ghaya as mere termination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection to the strict definition of ghaya (end) is answered by broadening the concept to include unintended termini.

Core Idea
Ghaya can simply mean where an action ends, not what is intended.

The previous lesson presented ghaya as an effect (ma'lul) and intended by the agent. But what about cases where the endpoint is eternal, accidental, or involuntary? This objection raises three such exceptions. The answer is elegantly simple: the term ghaya is used in a broader sense that does not require intention. By redefining ghaya as the mere termination point, the earlier claim about ghaya being an effect is preserved for the broader usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Note the three examples given: eternal ghaya (God), accidental ghaya (finding treasure), involuntary ghaya (stone falling).
These examples challenge the idea that ghaya must be an effect and intended.
2
Identify the core of the objection
The objector is claiming that the definition of ghaya as an intended effect is too narrow. List the three categories: eternal, unintended, involuntary.
The objection is not denying ghaya, but pointing out exceptions.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer distinguishes between two senses of ghaya. The broad sense is simply 'that to which the action ends.' The narrow sense includes intention. The answer concedes the examples but keeps the term in the broad sense.
This preserves the earlier point about ghaya being an effect when taken narrowly.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Why does this matter? It shows that the same term can be used analogically. In kalam, precision of language is key, but flexibility is sometimes needed to account for different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الغاية قد لا تكون معلولاً بل قديماً كما يقال: الواجب تعالى غاية الغايات، وقد لا يكون مقصوداً للفاعل، وإن كان مختاراً كالعثور على الكنز في حفر البئر، وقد لا يكون للفاعل قصد واختيار كغاية الحركات الغير الإرادية، مثل الوصول إلى الأرض كهبوط الحجر. قلنا: قد تطلق الغاية على ما ينتهي إليه الفعل، وإن لم يكن مقصوداً. وبهذا الاعتبار أثبتوا للقوى الطبيعية والأسباب الاتفاقية غايات.
If it is said: The ghaya may not be an effect, but rather eternal, as is said: the Necessary Exalted is the end of ends; and it may not be intended by the agent, even if he is free, like stumbling upon a treasure when digging a well; and there may be no purpose or choice by the agent, like the end of involuntary movements, such as reaching the ground when a stone falls. We say: The term ghaya is sometimes applied to that to which the action ends, even if it is not intended. And in this sense they affirmed ends for natural forces and accidental causes.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous account of ghaya. The objector lists three exceptions to the idea that ghaya is an effect and intended: (1) God is called 'ghayat al-ghayat' (end of ends) yet He is not an effect. (2) Unintentional outcomes like finding treasure while digging. (3) Involuntary movements like a stone falling. The answer redefines ghaya broadly as simply the termination point of an action, not necessarily intended. Based on this broader sense, ends are affirmed for natural forces and accidental causes.

Key Terms
غاية الغايات
غاية اتفاقية
قصد
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghaya always implies a goal or purpose pursued by an agent.
Correction: Ghaya can simply mean the endpoint of an action, even without intention or choice, as with natural phenomena.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three examples given in the objection?
A) God as end of ends
B) Finding treasure while digging a well
C) The falling of a stone
D) The carpenter's sitting

Key Takeaway
The term ghaya is used in two senses: the broad sense of mere termination, which applies even to unintended and involuntary phenomena, and the narrow sense of an intended goal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent situation where something good happened to you that you did not intend or plan. Did you recognize God's hand in it, or did you assume it was just luck? How does this lesson's concept of ghaya as mere termination challenge you to see unintended blessings as still having an end in God's plan?
?
You often set goals and intend outcomes. But what if some of God's purposes for your life are achieved through your unintended actions or even failures? Are you willing to let go of your own intended goals to submit to God's ultimate end? Identify one area where you need to surrender your intentions to His.
?
When you say 'God is the end of all ends,' do you actually live as if He is the endpoint of your desires and actions, or do you treat Him as a means to get something else? What would change today if you truly made Him your ultimate ghaya?

Essential vs. Accidental Ends in Causality
Classifying ends into essential and accidental based on causal regularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between an essential end (ghaya dhatiyya) and an accidental end (ghaya ittifaqiyya), and how the presence or absence of conditions affects causal classification.

Core Idea
A cause's regularity determines whether its effect is essential or accidental.

We often assume that a cause always produces its effect, but in reality, some effects depend on additional conditions. This lesson clarifies that when an effect follows a cause always or for the most part, it is an essential end; otherwise, it is accidental. The passage provides a precise definition and then verifies it by analyzing the role of external conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once silently. Then read the translation. Note how the author first gives a simple definition and then a more detailed verification.
Pay attention to the example of digging a well and finding treasure.
2
Identify the two categories of ends
Find where the passage defines essential end and accidental end. Notice the criterion: always or most of the time vs. otherwise.
The example illustrates the accidental end.
3
Analyze the verification section
The passage explains that a cause may require external conditions to produce its effect. Without those conditions, it is an accidental cause; with them, it becomes a full cause. Reflect on how this refines the initial definition.
Consider how the same effect can be essential relative to the full cause and accidental relative to the cause alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا: ما يتأدى إليه السبب إن كان تأديه دائماً أو أكثرياً فهي غاية ذاتية وإلا فاتفاقية كمن حفر بئراً فوجد كنزاً، وتحقيقه: أن العلة قد تتوقف عليتها على أمور خارجة عن ذاتها غير دائمة ولا أكثرية معها. فيقال لها بدون تلك الشرائط علة اتفاقية. فإن اتفق حصول تلك الشرائط معها ترتب المعلول عليها لا محالة، فيسمى ذلك المعلول باعتبار النسبة إلى العلة وحدها غاية اتفاقية. وإن كان باعتبار النسبة إليها مع جميع الشرائط غاية ذاتية.
And they said: What the cause leads to — if it leads to it always or for the most part — then it is an essential (dhatiyya) end; otherwise, it is accidental (ittifaqiyya), like someone who dug a well and found a treasure. And its verification: that the cause may depend in its causality upon matters external to itself that are not permanent or usually concomitant. So it is called, without those conditions, an accidental cause. If those conditions happen to coincide with it, the effect follows necessarily upon it. Then that effect is called, in relation to the cause alone, an accidental end; and in relation to it together with all conditions, an essential end.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage gives a formal classification of ends into essential (dhatiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya). An essential end is when the cause produces its effect always or for the most part. An accidental end occurs when the effect follows only if certain external conditions coincide, like finding treasure when digging (the digging alone does not always produce treasure). The passage then analyzes the conditions: a cause may need external conditions to be effective; without them it is an accidental cause; with them it becomes a full cause, and the resulting end is essential relative to the full cause, but accidental relative to the cause alone.

Key Terms
غاية ذاتية
غاية اتفاقية
علة اتفاقية
شرائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidental ends are uncaused or random.
Correction: Accidental ends are caused, but not by the cause alone; they require additional coinciding conditions.

Checkpoint
When is an effect considered an essential end (ghaya dhatiyya)?
A) When it is intended by the agent.
B) When it occurs always or for the most part after the cause.
C) When it is eternal.
D) When it is known by the mind.

Key Takeaway
An effect is an essential end if its cause produces it always or usually; otherwise it is accidental, but it can become essential if additional necessary conditions are included.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What hidden conditions might be necessary for that outcome? How does this shift your reliance from your own effort to the One who controls all conditions?

?

The Ash'ari Meaning of Cause: Customary Succession
Explaining that in Ash'ari theology, 'cause' means only customary succession, not real influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Ash'ari theology, 'cause' means only customary succession by Allah's permission, not real causal influence.

Core Idea
For Ash'aris, 'cause' is just God's custom—no created thing truly influences another.

This note clarifies a foundational Ash'ari doctrine: only Allah is the true creator. When we say fire causes burning, we mean that Allah customarily creates burning after fire contacts wood. The author explains this by contrasting with philosophers and others who attribute real influence to created causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two views
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the contrast between those who believe some possible things influence others and the Ash'ari view that all is ascribed to Allah initially.
Pay attention to the phrase 'على رأي من يجعل بعض الممكنات مؤثرا' vs. 'على رأى القائلين بإسناد الكل إلى الله'.
2
Understand the Ash'ari definition of cause
The passage defines 'cause' for Ash'aris: it is customary that Allah creates the effect after the cause, without any real influence. Focus on the phrase 'جرى العادة بإذن الله تعالى يخلق ذلك الشيء عقب ذلك الممكن'.
3
Examine the examples of fire and Zayd's eating
The examples illustrate that the same effect could follow different antecedents, but only the customary one is called 'cause'. This shows it is not a necessary connection.
Fire is called cause of burning, but if burning occurred after water contact, water would not be called cause.
4
Reflect on the implication for everyday language
Muslims still use causal language, but it carries no metaphysical necessity. What does this imply about how we should understand statements like 'fire burns'?
This connects to the broader Islamic theology of occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال) تنبيه: لما كان الموجد عندنا هو الله تعالى وحده كان معنى العلة الممكنات ما جرت العادة بخلق الشيء عقيبه. من أكثر الأحكام السابقة للعلة الفاعلية لمعنى المؤثر كالانقسام إلى البسيطة والمركبة وإلى الكلية والجزئية وبكونها معلولاً لأمر آخر، ولكونها متناهية الآثار إلى غير ذلك إنما هي على رأي من يجعل بعض الممكنات مؤثراً في البعض كالفلاسفة وكثير من المليين. وأما على رأى القائلين بإسناد الكل إلى الله تعالى ابتداء فمعنى علية الممكن للشيء جرى العادة بإذن الله تعالى يخلق ذلك الشيء عقب ذلك الممكن بحيث يتبادر إلى الذهن أن وجوده موقوف على وجوده بحيث يصلح أن يقال: وجد فوجد من غير أن يكون له تأثير فيه. فعلة الاحتراق تكون هي النار لا الماء، وإن وجد عقيب ملامستها، وعلة أكل زيد لا يكون شرب عمرو وإن وجد عقيبه.
He said: A note: Since the Creator according to us is Allah alone, the meaning of a possible thing being a cause (illah) is that it is customary to create the thing after it. Most of the previous rulings concerning the efficient cause, regarding the meaning of 'influencer' (mu'aththir), such as its division into simple and compound, universal and particular, and its being an effect of something else, and its finite effects, etc., are only on the view of those who make some possible things influential on others, like the philosophers and many of the religious people. As for the view of those who ascribe all (al-kull) to Allah initially, the meaning of a possible thing being a cause for something is that, by the permission of Allah, it is customary that Allah creates that thing after that possible thing, such that it comes to mind that its existence is dependent on its existence, so that it may be said: 'it exists, so it exists' without it having any influence in it. Thus the cause of burning is the fire, not water, even if it occurs after contact with it, and the cause of Zayd's eating is not 'Amr's drinking, even if it occurs after it.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This 'tanbih' (note) addresses a key Ash'ari doctrine: only Allah is the true creator. When we say a possible thing (like fire) is a 'cause' of something (burning), it means merely that Allah customarily creates burning after fire contacts wood, not that fire has any real causal power. The author notes that many earlier discussions about efficient causes (division into simple/compound, etc.) presuppose that some created things can influence others—a view held by philosophers and many religious thinkers. But on the correct Ash'ari view, causality is mere habitual succession (al-'ada). The example illustrates: fire is called the cause of burning, but only because Allah creates burning after contact with fire, not because fire itself produces it. Similarly, Zayd's eating is not caused by 'Amr's drinking even if it follows it. This is the meaning of 'found so exists'.

Key Terms
موجد
علة
عادة
إذن الله
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'aris deny causality entirely.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm causality as customary succession (al-‘ādah) by Allah's permission; they deny necessary causal connection.

Checkpoint
According to this note, what does 'cause' (illah) mean for Ash'aris?
A) Real causal power in created things.
B) Customary succession by the permission of Allah.
C) A necessary connection.
D) A logical implication.

Key Takeaway
In Ash'ari theology, 'cause' means habitual succession by Allah's permission, not real causal power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Essential and Accidental Ends in Causality
Defines essential and accidental ends and explains the role of conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential and accidental ends in causality and the role of conditions in this classification.

Core Idea
A cause's effect is essential if always/mostly, accidental if not.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces a key distinction in the study of causality. The author explains how the same effect can be considered essential or accidental depending on whether we include the conditions that accompany the cause. Understanding this distinction helps clarify how theologians and philosophers analyze cause-effect relationships.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the author uses a definition, example, and then a verification (tahqiq).
2
Identify the rule for essential vs accidental ends
Find the sentence that states the rule: if the cause always or mostly leads to the effect, it's essential; otherwise accidental. Note the exception clause.
The example of digging a well and finding a treasure illustrates an accidental end.
3
Understand the verification (tahqiq)
The verification explains how conditions external to the cause affect classification. Without those conditions the cause is accidental; with all conditions, the cause's causality is complete and the effect becomes essential relative to the whole set.
This clarifies that 'accidental' does not mean uncaused but rather dependent on external factors.
4
Reflect on the example
Consider the well-digging example: digging a well is not normally for finding treasure; when treasure is found, it is accidental relative to digging alone but essential if the digging included the intention to find treasure? Think about how the conditions change the classification.
The example helps solidify the abstract concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا الاعتبار أثبتوا للقوى الطبيعية والأسباب الاتفاقية غايات. وقالوا: ما يتأدى إليه السبب إن كان تأديه دائماً أو أكثرياً فهي غاية ذاتية وإلا فاتفاقية كمن حفر بئراً فوجد كنزاً، وتحقيقه: أن العلة قد تتوقف عليتها على أمور خارجة عن ذاتها غير دائمة ولا أكثرية معها. فيقال لها بدون تلك الشرائط علة اتفاقية. فإن اتفق حصول تلك الشرائط معها ترتب المعلول عليها لا محالة، فيسمى ذلك المعلول باعتبار النسبة إلى العلة وحدها غاية اتفاقية. وإن كان باعتبار النسبة إليها مع جميع الشرائط غاية ذاتية.
By this consideration they affirmed purposes for natural forces and accidental causes. And they said: That to which the cause leads, if its leading is always or mostly, then it is an essential end; otherwise it is accidental, like one who digs a well and finds a treasure. Its verification: the cause may depend in its causality on matters external to its essence that are not permanent nor mostly with it. So it is called without those conditions an accidental cause. If those conditions happen to occur with it, the effect follows from it necessarily, so that effect is called, in relation to the cause alone, an accidental end. But if in relation to it together with all conditions, it is an essential end.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the distinction between essential and accidental ends (ghaya dhatiyya wa ghaya ittifaqiyya). The author begins by stating that the philosophers and theologians have affirmed purposes for natural forces and accidental causes based on this consideration. They then give the rule: if a cause always or mostly leads to its effect, that effect is an essential end; if not, it is accidental, illustrated by the example of digging a well and finding a treasure. The verification (tahqiq) explains that a cause may require external conditions for its causality; without those conditions it is an accidental cause. When those conditions occur, the effect necessarily follows, and that effect is accidental relative to the cause alone, but essential relative to the cause plus all conditions. This distinction is crucial for understanding causation in occasionalist versus naturalist frameworks.

Key Terms
غاية ذاتية
غاية اتفاقية
علة
شرائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidental means uncaused or random without any cause.
Correction: An accidental end is still caused, but the cause's connection to the effect is not regular (always or mostly) without the external conditions. When conditions occur, the effect follows necessarily, so it is caused.

Checkpoint

A) Whether the cause is natural or supernatural.
B) Whether the cause always or mostly leads to the effect.
C) Whether the effect is good or evil.
D) Whether the cause is simple or composite.

Key Takeaway
Whether an end is essential or accidental depends on the regularity of the cause-effect relationship and whether one includes the necessary conditions in the cause.

The Islamic View of Causality vs. the Philosophers' View
Distinguishes the Muslim occasionalist view from the philosophers' view of causal efficacy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic occasionalist view of causality and how it differs from the philosophical view of real causal efficacy.

Core Idea
In Islam, causes are mere habits; Allah alone is the true agent.

This passage is a clarifying note from Sanusi that cuts to the heart of a major theological divide: do created things have real causal power? For Muslims who uphold divine transcendence, the answer is no. The author explains what 'cause' really means in an Islamic framework and contrasts it with the views of philosophers and others.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to how Sanusi defines 'cause' for Muslims.
The passage begins with a prepositional phrase that sets the context.
2
Identify the two views
Notice that the passage contrasts two opinions: the Muslim occasionalist view and the view of those who attribute real influence to some possible things (philosophers and milliyyun). Underline the key phrases.
3
Analyze the examples
The examples of fire/water and Zayd/Amr illustrate that the customary sequence is what matters, not inherent power. Ask yourself: Why is fire the cause of burning and not water?
The examples show that the same effect can follow different antecedents, but only the one that is customary is called 'cause'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال) تنبيه: لما كان الموجد عندنا هو الله تعالى وحده كان معنى العلة الممكنات ما جرت العادة بخلق الشيء عقيبه. من أكثر الأحكام السابقة للعلة الفاعلية لمعنى المؤثر كالانقسام إلى البسيطة والمركبة وإلى الكلية والجزئية وبكونها معلولاً لأمر آخر، ولكونها متناهية الآثار إلى غير ذلك إنما هي على رأي من يجعل بعض الممكنات مؤثراً في البعض كالفلاسفة وكثير من المليين. وأما على رأي القائلين بإسناد الكل إلى الله تعالى ابتداء فمعنى علية الممكن للشيء جرى العادة بإذن الله تعالى يخلق ذلك الشيء عقب ذلك الممكن بحيث يتبادر إلى الذهن أن وجوده موقوف على وجوده بحيث يصلح أن يقال: وجد فوجد من غير أن يكون له تأثير فيه. فعلة الاحتراق تكون هي النار لا الماء، وإن وجد عقيب مماسستها. وعلة أكل زيد لا يكون شرب عمرو، وإن وجد عقيبه.
(He said) A note: Since the Creator according to us is God Almighty alone, the meaning of 'cause' for possible things is what customarily happens of creating the thing after it. Most of the previous judgments about the efficient cause—such as division into simple and composite, into universal and particular, its being an effect of another thing, its having finite effects, etc.—are only according to the view of those who make some possible things effective upon others, like the philosophers and many of the milliyyun (adherents of religions). As for the view of those who ascribe everything to God Almighty from the beginning, the meaning of a possible thing's being a cause of something is the customary practice by God's permission of creating that thing after that possible thing, such that it springs to the mind that its existence depends on its existence, so that it is appropriate to say: 'it existed, so it existed' without its having any effect in it. Thus the cause of burning is fire, not water, even if it occurs after its contact. And the cause of Zayd's eating is not 'Amr's drinking, even if it occurs after it.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbih) clarifying the difference between the Islamic occasionalist view of causality and the view of philosophers and some religious thinkers who attribute causal efficacy to created things. The author states that since for us the sole agent is Allah, the term 'cause' for possible things means nothing more than a regular sequence (customary practice) where Allah creates the effect after the cause. He then notes that most of the previous discussions about efficient causes—like their division into simple/composite, universal/particular, etc.—apply only on the view that some possible things have real influence, which is the position of philosophers and many milliyyun. On the other hand, those who ascribe all agency to Allah directly hold that the causality of a possible thing is merely a matter of divine custom: Allah creates the effect after the cause, so that one naturally thinks the effect depends on the cause, but the cause has no actual influence. Two examples are given: fire is the cause of burning, not water, even if contact with water follows; and Zayd's eating is the cause of his satiety, not 'Amr's drinking, even if it follows. This underscores the occasionalist position that habits (customs) are established by Allah, not inherent powers.

Key Terms
علة
ممكن
موجد
عادة الله
فلاسفة
مليون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Occasionalism denies any regularity in the world, making events random and unpredictable.
Correction: Occasionalism affirms regular sequences (customs) established by Allah, so the world is predictable, but these sequences are not necessary connections.

B) It is a necessary concomitant of the effect.
C) Allah's custom of creating the effect after it.
D) The effect emanates from it necessarily.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic theology, the causality of created things is nothing but divine custom; real agency belongs to Allah alone.

The First Argument for Composition as a Bond and Its Refutation
First argument and its refutation regarding composition as bond.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that composition is a real bond holding body parts together, and the refutation that denies its necessity by appealing to the will of a free agent.

Core Idea
Cohesion of body parts may be due to divine will, not just a physical bond.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents the first argument for composition (tā'līf) as a real bond that holds the parts of a body together. He then offers a refutation that challenges the necessity of this bond, suggesting an alternative cause: the will of the free agent. This lesson examines the logic of the argument and the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic meaning.
Notice the two main parts: the argument and the response.
2
Analyze the argument for composition as a bond
Identify the premises: difficulty of separation requires a bond; the only candidate is composition; composition must be a positive accident.
The argument attempts to prove composition is a real bond.
3
Understand the refutation
The response denies the necessity and proposes an alternative cause: the will of the free agent. This shows the argument is not conclusive.
Consider how this refutation opens the door to occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجزاء كثيرة كان بين كل جزأين تأليف مغاير للتأليف القائم بجزئين آخرين، أما الأول فلأن عسر انفكاك أجزاء الجسم لا بد أن يكون لرابطة، وليس إلا التأليف لأنه لم يحصل عند اجتماعها وصيرورتها جسماً آخر غيره فلا يكون عدمياً، بل ثبوتياً قائماً بشيئين ضرورة، ورد بالمنع لجواز أن يكون بسبب آخر كإرادة الفاعل المختار
Many parts between each two parts there is a composition different from the composition subsisting in two other parts. As for the first: because the difficulty of separating the parts of the body must be due to a bond, and it is nothing but composition, because it did not come about when they assembled and became another body other than it, so it is not non-existential, but rather existential subsisting in two things necessarily. It was responded to by denial, for it is possible that it be due to another cause such as the will of the free agent.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the reality of composition (tā'līf) as a bond holding the parts of a body together. The author states that many parts have a specific composition between each pair, distinct from the composition between other pairs. The first point (الأول) is that the difficulty of separating the parts of a body must be due to a bond. This bond cannot be anything other than composition itself, because when the parts come together to form a body, no other thing arises besides this composition. Therefore, composition is not a mere non-existential (ʿadamī) but a positive (thubūtī) accident subsisting in two things. Then the response (رد) objects by denying this necessity: it is possible that the cohesion is due to another cause, such as the will of the freely acting agent (irādat al-fāʿil al-mukhtār). So the argument that composition must be the bond is not conclusive.

Key Terms
تأليف (tā'līf)
عدم (ʿadam)
ثبوت (thubūt)
فاعل مختار (free agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Composition must be a real physical bond because body parts are difficult to separate.
Correction: The refutation shows that the difficulty of separation could be due to the will of the free agent, not necessarily a physical bond.

Checkpoint
What is the response to the first argument that composition must be the bond?
A) It is possible that the bond is the will of the Agent
B) Composition is not necessary
C) The argument is accepted
D) Bodies do not need a bond

Key Takeaway
The first argument for composition as a bond is not definitive; the bond could be the will of the free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you habitually attribute the stability and cohesion of the world around you to natural laws and physical properties, or do you see the will of Allah as the direct cause? What would change if you consciously remembered that every bond is held by His will?
?
The refutation in this lesson shows that the assumed necessity of physical composition can be overturned by the possibility of divine will. Is there any area of your life where you have assumed a cause is necessary, when in fact Allah's will could be the real cause? How can you train yourself to leave room for that possibility?

The Second Argument and Its Rebuttal
Presents the second argument and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that composition cannot subsist in more than two parts and the response that the remaining composition may not be identical.

Core Idea
Removing a part does not necessarily preserve the same composition.

The author presents a second argument against the possibility of composition subsisting in more than two parts. The argument claims that if composition were in three parts, removing one would annihilate it, but composition remains between the other two — a contradiction. However, the response challenges the assumption that the remaining composition is the same.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: argument, then response.
The argument is based on the principle that the state is annihilated when its locus is annihilated.
2
Identify the argument's key premise
The argument says that if composition subsisted in three parts, removal of one part would annihilate the composition because the locus is all three parts. Ask yourself: is that premise always true?
3
Understand the response
The response denies that the composition remaining after removal is identical to the original. It could be a new composition. See how this undermines the argument's conclusion.
This introduces the concept of non-identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثانى فلأنه لو قام بأكثر من جزءين كالثلاثة مثلا لانعدم بانعدام أحد الأجزاء، ضرورة انعدام الحال بانعدام المحل، الذي هو جميع الأجزاء، واللازم باطل ضرورة بقاء التأليف فيما بين الجزأين الباقيين، ورد بأنا لا نسلم أن التأليف الباقي بين الجزأين هو بعينه التأليف القائم بالثلاثة، لم لا يجوز أن ينعدم ذلك، ويحدث هذا ؟ فإن قيل قيام العرض الواحد بالكثير مما قال به الفلاسفة كالوحدة بالعشرة الواحدة والتثليث لمجموع الأضلاع الثلاثة المحيطة بسطح، والحياة ببنية متجزئة إلى أعضاء، والقيام بمجموع أجزاء زيد. قلنا : المتنازع فيه هو أن يكون العرض القائم بمحل هو بعينه القائم بالمحل الآخر إلا أن يكون العرض الواحد قائماً بمجموع شيئين صاراً بالاجتماع محلا واحداً له، كما في هذه الصورة والظاهر أن مراد أبي هاشم أيضاً هذا المعنى، إلا أنه لم يجوز القيام بما فوق الاثنين لما ذكر من لزوم انعدام التأليف عند إزالة أحد الأجزاء من الاجتماع، وكأنه يدعى القطع ببقاء التأليف دون زوال تأليف وحدوث آخر
As for the second: because if it (composition) subsisted in more than two parts, like three for example, it would be annihilated by the annihilation of one of the parts, due to the necessity of the annihilation of the state by the annihilation of the locus, which is all the parts. But the consequent is false due to the necessity of the remaining of composition between the two remaining parts. It was responded to by saying: we do not accept that the composition remaining between the two parts is identical to the composition that subsisted in the three; why is it not possible that that composition is annihilated and this one comes to be? Then if it is said: the subsistence of a single accident in many is something asserted by the philosophers, like unity in ten, trinity in the sum of three sides enclosing a surface, and life in a structure divisible into organs, and subsistence in the sum of Zayd's parts. We say: the point of dispute is that an accident subsisting in one substrate is identical to the one subsisting in another substrate, unless a single accident subsists in the sum of two things that have become by aggregation one substrate for it, as in this case. And it appears that the intention of Abu Hashim is also this meaning, except that he did not allow subsistence in more than two, due to the mentioned necessary consequence of the annhilation of composition upon removal of one part from the aggregation, and it is as if he claims certainty of the remaining of composition without the removal of one composition and the occurrence of another.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on composition (tā'līf) by presenting the second argument (الثانى). The argument states that if composition were to subsist in more than two parts, such as three, then when one part is removed, the composition would be annihilated because the state (composition) is annihilated with the annihilation of its locus, which is all the parts together. However, this consequence is false because composition remains between the remaining two parts. The response (رد) is that we do not accept that the composition remaining after removal is identical to the one that subsisted in the three; it could be that the original composition is annihilated and a new one comes into existence. Then comes an objection (فإن قيل) from the opponent who appeals to the philosophers' examples of a single accident subsisting in many things, such as unity in ten, trinity in three sides of a triangle, and life in divisible body parts. The author replies (قلنا) by clarifying the point of dispute: the issue is whether an accident subsisting in one location is identical to the one subsisting in another location. However, it is possible for a single accident to subsist in the aggregate of two things that together form one substrate, as in the examples. The author then explains that this is likely what Abu Hashim meant, except that Abu Hashim did not allow subsistence in more than two, due to the necessity of composition's annihilation when one part is removed. Abu Hashim seems to claim certainty that composition remains without one composition being replaced by another. The author is analyzing the positions.

Key Terms
تأليف (tā'līf)
عرض (ʿaraḍ)
محل (maḥall)
قيام (qiyām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If composition remains after removal of a part, it must be the same composition as before.
Correction: It is possible that the original composition is annihilated and a new composition occurs, so the identity is not guaranteed.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the second argument presented in this passage?
A) Composition cannot subsist in more than two parts because removal would annihilate it, yet composition remains between remaining parts.
B) Composition can subsist in three parts because the response is invalid.
C) The philosophers' examples show it is possible for an accident to subsist in many.
D) Abu Hashim's view is correct.

Key Takeaway
The second argument against composition in many parts is rebutted by denying that the remaining composition is identical to the original.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a relationship or state in your life that you assumed remained identical after a significant change. Were you correct? How does this lesson's denial of the identity of compositions after removal force you to reconsider your assumption?

When have you insisted that something was 'still the same' despite evidence of transformation? What would it cost you to admit that the old has passed away and something new has come to be, just as the composition between two parts is not the same as the one between three?
?
If the composition after removal is not necessarily the same, then what else in your spiritual life might you be mistakenly assuming persists unchanged? How can you cultivate the humility to recognize when something has been annihilated and replaced, rather than clinging to the illusion of continuity?
