The Objection from Philosophical Examples
Response to the objection from philosophical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dispute is resolved by clarifying that a single accident can subsist in an aggregate of many parts that form one substrate.

Core Idea
Unity in many is possible if the many become one substrate.

The opponent raises examples from philosophy where a single accident, such as unity or life, seems to subsist in many things. The author replies by specifying the point of dispute: whether the accident in one substrate is identical to that in another. In these examples, the many parts together form a single substrate, so the accident is one in one locus, not many in many loci.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the opponent cites three examples from the philosophers.
The examples are: unity in ten, trinity in three sides of a triangle, life in divisible bodily structure.
2
Identify the author's rebuttal strategy
The author reframes the dispute: it is about identity of an accident across separate substrates. In the examples, the many things become one substrate by aggregation, not separate substrates.
This is a key distinction.
3
Consider Abu Hashim's position
The author notes that Abu Hashim intended a similar meaning, but only allowed subsistence in up to two because he thought removal of one part would annihilate composition without replacement.
Compare with the earlier rebuttal.

The Impossibility of an Accident Transferring: Three Proofs and Responses
Presents three proofs that an accident cannot transfer and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why accidents cannot transfer between substrates, and how the three proofs and their refutations are articulated in the kalam tradition.

Core Idea
An accident's existence is its inherence; perceived transfer is actually the creation of a similar accident.

This investigation delves into a classic theological debate: can an accident move from one substrate to another? The author presents the consensus that it cannot, and then explains why common examples like the spread of smell are mistaken. The passage then outlines three proofs for the impossibility, each followed by a counter-response. Following the arguments carefully reveals the sophisticated reasoning behind this principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the consensus and the key clarification
Read the opening statement that accidents cannot transfer because their existence is their inherence. Then note the clarification: apparent transfer (like smell) is actually the creation of a similar accident in a new substrate.
This distinction is crucial for the entire argument.
2
Examine the first proof and its response
The first proof defines transfer as spatial movement, which accidents cannot do since they are non-spatial. The response shifts the definition for accidents: transfer means obtaining in a substrate after another, not spatial movement.
3
Analyze the second proof on individuation and the response
The second proof argues that an accident's individuation depends on its substrate, so it cannot persist without it. The response denies that the relation to all substrates is equal, especially for a free agent (mukhtār).
This touches on causality and divine action.
4
Study the third proof and its response
The third proof presents a dilemma: the needed substrate is either determinate or indeterminate. The response resolves it by saying the accident is determinate by a determination like the place of a body.
5
Reflect on the overall argumentative structure
Consider how the author presents consensus, clarifies a potential counterexample, then systematically gives proofs and responses. This is typical of kalam texts: presenting the strongest objections and refuting them.
Notice that the responses are not necessarily accepted; this is a dialectical presentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث العرض لا ينتقل من محل إلى آخر قال : اتفقوا على امتناع انتقال العرض، لأن وجوده في نفسه هو وجوده محله، فما يتوهم من انتقال الكيفيات كالروايح وغيرها حدوث للمثل في المجاور. واحتجوا بوجوه . في الأول : أن الانتقال هو الحصول في الحيز بعد الحصول في آخر فلا يتصور في غير المتحيز ورد بأن ذلك في الجوهر، وأما في العرض فالحصول في محل بعد الحصول في آخر . الثاني : أن تشخصه ليس لماهيته وإلا انحصر في شخص، ولا لما يحل فيه وإلا لدار ولا المنفصل عنه ، لأن نسبته إلى الكل على السواء، ولا لهويته، لأنها لا تتقدم الشخص بل لمحله فلا تبقى بدونه. ورد بمنع استواء النسبة سيما في المختار . الثالث: أن محله المحتاج إليه أما المعين فلا يفارقه أو المبهم فلا يوجد، ورد بأنه المعين بتعين ما كحيز الجسم .
The Third Investigation: The accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. He said: They agreed on the impossibility of the transfer of an accident, because its existence in itself is its existence in its substrate. So what is imagined of the transfer of qualities like smells and others is the occurrence of the like in the neighboring [substrate]. And they adduced proofs. The first: that transfer is obtaining in a place after obtaining in another, so it is not conceivable in what is not spatial. It was responded to that this is in the substance, but as for the accident, it is obtaining in a substrate after obtaining in another. The second: that its individuation is not due to its essence, otherwise it would be restricted to one individual; nor due to what it inheres in, otherwise there would be circularity; nor due to what is separate from it, because its relation to all is equal; nor due to its identity, because it does not precede the individual, but rather, it is for its substrate, so it does not remain without it. It was responded to by denying the equality of relation, especially in the case of the freely choosing [agent]. The third: that its substrate which it needs is either determinate, then it does not leave it, or indeterminate, then it does not exist. It was responded to that it is determinate by a determination like the place of the body.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage introduces the third investigation (المبحث الثالث) concerning the impossibility of an accident (ʿaraḍ) transferring from one substrate to another. The author states that there is consensus on the impossibility, because the accident's existence is precisely its inherence in a substrate. Thus, any perceived transfer, like that of smells, is actually the occurrence of a similar accident in a neighboring location. Then three proofs are presented, each followed by a response. The first proof: transfer is defined as obtaining in a spatial location after another, so it is inconceivable for non-spatial entities like accidents. The response: this definition applies to substances, not accidents; for accidents, transfer means obtaining in a substrate after another. The second proof: the individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) of an accident cannot be due to its essence (otherwise it would be limited to one instance), nor due to its substrate (circular), nor due to something separate (since relation to all is equal), nor due to its identity (since identity does not precede the individual but depends on the substrate). Thus it cannot persist without its substrate. The response denies that the relation is equal, especially in the case of a free agent (al-mukhtār) where the accident's individuation may depend on the agent's choice. The third proof: the accident's needed substrate is either determinate (muʿayyan) – then it cannot leave it, or indeterminate (mubham) – then it does not exist. The response: it is determinate by a determination, such as the place (ḥayyiz) of the body, so it can be specific.

Key Terms
عرض (ʿaraḍ)
انتقال (intiqāl)
محل (maḥall)
تشخص (tashakhkhuṣ)
ماهية (māhiyya)
مختار (mukhtār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The spread of a smell is an accident actually moving from one place to another.
Correction: The author clarifies that it is the creation of a similar accident in the new location, not the transfer of the original accident.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the second proof, what is the problem with the claim that the accident's relation to all substrates is equal?
A) It is false because accidents can transfer
B) It is denied, especially in the case of a free agent
C) It is accepted as a necessary truth
D) It only applies to substances

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of accident transfer is defended through three proofs addressing spatiality, individuation, and substrate determination, each answered in a dialectical manner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perceive your own emotions changing, do you assume they are yours to move and control, or do you recognize them as accidents created in you moment by moment by the Creator (the Mukhtār)? How does this lesson challenge your sense of ownership over your own states?

The passage says that what seems like transfer (e.g., a smell moving) is actually the creation of a similar accident in a new location. Can you think of a time you mistakenly attributed continuity to a quality that was actually being re-created? How does this affect your trust in your own perceptions?
?
The proof about individuation says an accident's identity is tied to its substrate. What are you investing your identity in? Are you treating your accidental qualities (status, moods, skills) as if they define you permanently, when in reality they are momentary? What would it mean to see them as gifts that are re-created each moment?

The Two Proofs Against an Accident Inhering in Another Accident
Two proofs that an accident cannot inhere in another accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments the mutakallimun use to prove that an accident cannot inhere in another accident and must instead inhere directly in a substance.

Core Idea
An accident cannot inhere in another accident because it would be arbitrary and must terminate in a substance.

The mutakallimun, in their quest to understand the nature of accidents, argued that an accident cannot inhere in another accident. They provided two proofs to support this claim. The first proof appeals to priority and non-arbitrariness, while the second proof relies on the necessity of a substance as the ultimate substrate. In this lesson, we will examine both proofs carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage covering both proofs
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation, focusing on the logical flow of the two arguments.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the phrase 'ليس أولى' which signals the first proof.
2
Understand the first proof
Identify the argument that if accidents could inhere in each other, there would be no reason to choose one accident over another as a substrate; hence direct inherence in the substance is more entitled.
3
Understand the second proof
Notice the second proof: if an accident inheres in another accident, there must be a substance at the end of the chain because an accident cannot subsist by itself. Therefore, all accidents should inhere directly in that substance.
This proof relies on the 'impossibility of an accident subsisting by itself.'
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the mutakallimun conclude that direct inherence is more logical—because the substance is 'more worthy' as a substrate and the meaning of inherence is 'following in spatial domain.'
This establishes a hierarchy: substance (self-subsisting) → accident (dependent).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمتحيز بالذات ليس أولى من قيام الكل بذلك الجوهر. بحيث يصير ذلك الثاني : أنه لو قام عرض بعرض فلا بد في الآخرة من جوهر ينتهي إليه سلسلة الأعراض ضرورة امتناع قيام العرض بنفسه، وحينئذ فقيام بعض الأعراض بالبعض ليس أولى من قيام الكل بذلك الجوهر، بل هذا أولى، لأن القائم بنفسه أحق بأن يكون محلاً مقوماً للحال، ولأن الكل في حيز الجوهر تبعاً له، وهو معنى القيام. واعترض على الوجهين بأنا لا نسلم أن معنى قيام الشيء بالشيء التبعية في التحيز، بل معناه اختصاص الشي بالشيء نعتاً له وهو منعوت به كاختصاص البياض بالجسم لا الجسم بالمكان، والقيام بهذا المعنى لا يختص بالمتحيز كما في صفات الله تعالى عند المتكلمين وصفات الجواهر المجردة عند الفلاسفة فضلاً عن أن القيام بالمتحيز لا بالتبعية ثم انتهاء قيام العرض إلى الجوهر مما لا نزاع فيه، إلا أنه لا يوجب قيام الكل به لجواز أن يكون الاختصاص الناعت فيما بين بعض الأعراض بأن يكون عرضاً نعتاً لعرض لا للجوهر الذي إليه الانتهاء كالسرعة للحركة والملاسة للسطح والاستقامة للخط، فإن المنعوت حقيقة بهذه الأعراض هي تلك لا الجسم فلهذا جوزت الفلاسفة قيام العرض بالعرض وزعموا أن النقطة عرض قائم بالخط، والخط بالسطح، بمعنى أن ذا النقطة هو الخط، وذا الخط هو السطح، لا الجسم، ومن القائلين بجواز قيام العرض بالعرض من بالغ في ذلك وتمادى في الباطل، حتى زعم أن كلا من الوحدة والوجود عرض قائم بمحله. فوحدة العرض ووجوده يكون من قيام العرض بالعرض. وأجاب المتكلمون بأن مثل النقطة والخط عدمي، ولو سلم فمن الجواهر لا الأعراض على ما سيجيء، ومثل الملاسة والاستقامة على تقدير كونه وجودياً إنما يقوم بالجسم، وبأن السرعة والبطء ليس عرضاً زائداً على الحركة قائماً بها، بل الحركة أمر ممتد يتخلله سكنات أقل، أو أكثر باعتبارها سريعة أو بطيئة، ولو سلم أن البطء ليس لتخلل السكنات فطبقات الحركات أنواع مختلفة والسرعة والبطء عائد إلى الذاتيات دون العرضيات أو هما من الاعتبارات اللاحقة للحركة بحسب الإضافة إلى حركة أخرى بقطع المسافة المعينة في زمان أقل أو أكثر، ولهذا يختلف باختلاف الإضافة فتكون السريعة بطيئة بالنسبة إلى الأسرع وبالجملة فليس هناك عرضي هو الحركة، وآخر هو السرعة أو البطء، وأما الوحدة والوجود فقد سبق أن الوحدة اعتبار عقلي بل عدمي وأن الوجود في الخارج نفس الماهية، أو هو من الاعتبارات العقلية أو واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، وفي الجملة فجعله من قبيل الأعراض خطأ فاحش لا ينبغي أن يقول به المحصل فإن من شأن العرض أن يفتقر في التقوم إلى المحل ويستغني عنه المحل.
And a spatially located entity (al-mutahayyiz) by essence is not more entitled than that the whole inhere in that substance. Such that the second [proof] becomes: if an accident were to inhere in an accident, there must necessarily at the end be a substance at which the chain of accidents terminates, due to the impossibility of an accident subsisting by itself. Then the inherence of some accidents in others is not more entitled than the inherence of all in that substance; rather this is more entitled, because that which subsists by itself is more worthy to be a constitutive substrate for the state, and because the whole is in the domain of the substance as a follower of it, and that is the meaning of inherence. And an objection was raised against both ways: we do not accept that the meaning of a thing's inherence in a thing is following in spatial location; rather its meaning is the specification of a thing by a thing as an attribute for it, and it is described by it, like the specification of whiteness to a body, not the body to a place. And inherence in this sense is not specific to the spatially located, as in the attributes of God Almighty according to the mutakallimun and the attributes of immaterial substances according to the philosophers, and even more so that inherence is in the spatially located not by way of followership. Then the termination of an accident's inherence to a substance is undisputed, but it does not necessitate the inherence of all in it, because it is possible that the attributive specification is among some accidents such that an accident is an attribute for an accident, not for the substance to which the termination is, like speed for motion, smoothness for surface, and straightness for line. For the entity truly described by these accidents are those, not the body. Hence the philosophers permitted the inherence of an accident in an accident, and they claimed that the point is an accident inhering in the line, and the line in the surface, meaning that the possessor of the point is the line, and the possessor of the line is the surface, not the body. And among those who said that the inherence of an accident in an accident is permissible, there is one who went to extremes in that and persisted in falsehood, until he claimed that both unity and existence are accidents inhering in their subject. So the unity of the accident and its existence would be from the inherence of an accident in an accident. And the mutakallimun answered: that such as the point and line are non-existent, and if admitted, they are from substances not accidents as will come; and such as smoothness and straightness, if they are existent, they inhere in the body; and that speed and slowness are not an additional accident inhering in motion, but motion is an extended thing interspersed with fewer or more rests, by virtue of which it is called fast or slow; and if it is admitted that slowness is not due to interspersed rests, then the levels of motions are different types and speed and slowness pertain to essentialities not accidentalities, or they are among the considerations attached to motion according to its relation to another motion by traversing a given distance in less or more time. Thus it varies with variation of relation, so that the fast becomes slow relative to the faster. In sum, there is not there an accidental which is motion and another which is speed or slowness. As for unity and existence, it has preceded that unity is a mental consideration, rather non-existent, and that existence in the external is the essence itself, or it is among mental considerations or an intermediary between existent and non-existent. In sum, making it from the category of accidents is a gross error that the accomplished person should not state, because the nature of an accident is that it depends for subsistence on a substrate and the substrate is independent of it.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs by the mutakallimun that an accident cannot inhere in another accident, followed by an objection from the philosophers and the mutakallimun's rebuttal. The first proof argues that if all accidents inhere in a substance, there is no reason to prioritize one accident over another to inhere in a different accident rather than directly in the substance. The second proof posits that if an accident inheres in another accident, the chain of inherence must terminate in a substance because an accident cannot subsist by itself; therefore, it is more coherent for all accidents to inhere directly in that substance. The objection redefines 'inherence' not as spatial dependence but as attributive specification, citing God's attributes and immaterial substances as examples where inherence occurs without spatial location. The objector then gives examples like speed in motion, smoothness in surface, and straightness in line, claiming these are accidents inhering in other accidents, and even extends this to unity and existence. The mutakallimun respond by denying the existence of point and line as accidents (they are non-existent or substances), reasserting that smoothness and straightness inhere in the body, analyzing speed and slowness as not additional accidents but intrinsic or relational aspects of motion, and rejecting unity and existence as accidents (unity is mental, existence is the essence or mental). Thus they uphold that the original principle—that an accident inheres only in a substance—remains intact.

Key Terms
متحيز
جوهر
عرض
قيام العرض بالعرض
تبعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the second proof?
A) Accidents are eternal.
B) An accident cannot subsist by itself.
C) Substances are composed of accidents.
D) Inherence is a mental concept.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun prove that accidents must inhere directly in a substance, not in other accidents, because a chain would be arbitrary and must ultimately rest on a substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, you constantly rely on things that themselves depend on others. Pick one such dependency (a relationship, a possession, a skill) and trace its chain of support. Does it ultimately rest on anything that is self-subsisting in the way a substance is? What does that reveal about where you should place your ultimate trust?
?
Consider a quality about yourself that you think defines you — like intelligence, kindness, or reputation. These are accidents in the metaphysical sense: they depend on you. But do you treat them as if they were self-subsisting? How would your priorities shift if you saw every such quality as a 'dependent accident' needing a deeper foundation?
?
If you were to honestly admit that most things you rely on are like accidents — temporary and dependent — what would it cost you to anchor your life instead on the One who truly subsists by Himself? Are you willing to examine where your sense of stability really lies?

Objection and Response: Redefining Inherence and Defending the Principle
Objection to the proofs and the mutakallimun's detailed refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers objected to the mutakallimun's proofs by redefining inherence as attributive specification, and how the mutakallimun responded by analyzing each alleged example.

Core Idea
The objection changes the meaning of inherence, but the mutakallimun show that each example either fails or does not involve two accidents.

After the mutakallimun presented their proofs, the philosophers raised an objection by redefining the concept of inherence. They argued that inherence does not require spatial dependence but rather attributive specification, citing examples like speed in motion and smoothness in surface. In this lesson, we will examine the objection and the mutakallimun's detailed refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the redefinition of inherence
Note how the objector redefines inherence as 'attributive specification' rather than 'spatial followership.' This is key to the entire disagreement.
The objection uses examples from attributes of God to show inherence without spatial location.
2
Understand the philosophers' examples
Identify the examples given: point in line, line in surface, smoothness in surface, straightness in line, speed in motion, and unity/existence as accidents.
These are meant to prove that accidents can inhere in other accidents.
3
Trace the mutakallimun's refutation of point and line
Note that the response argues that point and line are non-existent or are substances, not accidents.
4
Trace the refutation of smoothness, straightness, speed, and slowness
The mutakallimun state that smoothness/straightness inhere in the body directly, and speed/slowness are not additional accidents but are properties of motion itself or relational considerations.
5
Trace the refutation of unity and existence
They argue that unity is a mental consideration (or non-existent) and existence is the essence itself or a mental consideration, not an accident.
The passage concludes that making them accidents is a gross error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمتحيز بالذات ليس أولى من قيام الكل بذلك الجوهر. بحيث يصير ذلك الثاني : أنه لو قام عرض بعرض فلا بد في الآخرة من جوهر ينتهي إليه سلسلة الأعراض ضرورة امتناع قيام العرض بنفسه، وحينئذ فقيام بعض الأعراض بالبعض ليس أولى من قيام الكل بذلك الجوهر، بل هذا أولى، لأن القائم بنفسه أحق بأن يكون محلاً مقوماً للحال، ولأن الكل في حيز الجوهر تبعاً له، وهو معنى القيام. واعترض على الوجهين بأنا لا نسلم أن معنى قيام الشيء بالشيء التبعية في التحيز، بل معناه اختصاص الشي بالشيء نعتاً له وهو منعوت به كاختصاص البياض بالجسم لا الجسم بالمكان، والقيام بهذا المعنى لا يختص بالمتحيز كما في صفات الله تعالى عند المتكلمين الأعراض بأن يكون عرضاً نعتاً لعرض لا للجوهر الذي إليه الانتهاء كالسرعة للحركة والملاسة للسطح والاستقامة للخط، فإن المنعوت حقيقة بهذه الأعراض هي تلك لا الجسم فلهذا جوزت الفلاسفة قيام العرض بالعرض And a spatially located entity (al-mutahayyiz) by essence is not more entitled than that the whole inhere in that substance. Commentary
This passage presents two proofs by the mutakallimun that an accident cannot inhere in another accident, followed by an objection from the philosophers and the mutakallimun's rebuttal. The first proof argues that if all accidents inhere in a substance, there is no reason to prioritize one accident over another to inhere in a different accident rather than directly in the substance. The second proof posits that if an accident inheres in another accident, the chain of inherence must terminate in a substance because an accident cannot subsist by itself; therefore, it is more coherent for all accidents to inhere directly in that substance. The objection redefines 'inherence' not as spatial dependence but as attributive specification, citing God's attributes and immaterial substances as examples where inherence occurs without spatial location. The objector then gives examples like speed in motion, smoothness in surface, and straightness in line, claiming these are accidents inhering in other accidents, and even extends this to unity and existence. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the mutakallimun deny that accidents can be attributes of each other at all.
Correction: The mutakallimun only deny that an accident can subsist through another accident (i.e., spatial followership), but they allow attributive specification where an accident describes another accident without subsistence.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun's response, why is speed not considered an accident inhering in motion?
A) Because speed is a substance.
B) Because speed is not an additional accident but a property of motion itself.
C) Because speed is a mental consideration only.
D) Because speed inheres directly in the body.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun refute each alleged case of an accident inhering in another accident, showing that either the supposed accident is not an accident, or it inheres directly in the substance, or it is not a separate entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector redefined inherence to win his argument. Are you alert to how you or others might shift definitions to make a point about Allah's attributes? Where in your own thinking do you need to hold firm to established meanings?
?
The mutakallimun insisted that speed is not an accident added to motion, but motion itself. In your spiritual life, do you treat your good deeds as extra 'add-ons' to yourself, or do you see them as the very movement of your soul towards Allah? What would change if you saw them as essential to who you are becoming?
?
Unity and existence are said to be mental considerations, not accidents. When you reflect on your own existence, do you ever imagine it as something added to you? How does this lesson's call to see existence as essence reshape your gratitude to the One who grants you existence every moment?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Second Proof: Impossibility of Removal of Persistent Accident
Demonstrates impossibility of removing persistent accident via exhaustive cause analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if an accident could persist, its removal would be impossible, thereby supporting the Ash'ari position that accidents cannot endure.

Core Idea
The removal of a persistent accident is impossible because every possible cause fails logically.

The second proof in this section tackles a key question: if accidents could persist, could they later cease? The author systematically considers every possible cause for removal, showing each leads to contradiction or infinite regress. This passage demonstrates the rigorous application of rational judgment in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the logical flow come through.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the overall claim
The passage starts with a conditional: if an accident persisted, its removal would be impossible. This is the claim to be proven.
3
Trace the breakdown of possible causes
The author lists four possible causes for removal: the accident's own essence, removal of a condition, occurrence of an opposite, or an agent. Note each one.
4
Examine why each cause fails
For each possible cause, understand the reasoning why it cannot be the cause: self-non-existence contradiction, infinite regress, circularity and symmetry, and non-existence as non-effect.
Pay attention to the two sub-reasons for the opposite cause.
5
Understand the conclusion
Since all possible causes are invalid, removal is impossible. This contradicts observed cessation of accidents, so the premise (persistence) must be false.
Consider how this supports the Ash'ari doctrine that accidents do not persist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو بقي لامتنع زواله ، واللازم ظاهر البطلان . وجه اللزوم : أنه لو أمكن زواله بعد البقاء لكان زواله حادثاً مفتقراً إلى سبب فسببه إما نفس ذاته فيمتنع وجوده ضرورة أن ما يكون عدمه مقتضي ذاته لم يوجد أصلاً. وأما زوال شرط من شرائط الوجود فينقل الكلام إلى زوال ذلك الشرط ويتسلسل ضرورة أنه يكون لزوال شرط له وهلم جرا، وإما طريان ضد وهو باطل لوجهين : أحدهما : لزوم الدور فإن طريان أحد الضدين على المحل مشروط بزوال الآخر، وهو موقوف عليه، فلو توقف زوال الآخر على طريانه كان دوراً. وثانيهما : أن التضاد والتنافي إنما هو من الجانبين فدفع الطارىء للباقي ليس أولى من دفع الباقي إياه بل الدفع أهون من الرفع، لأن فيما يرفع قوة استقرار وسابقه ثبات لا تكون فيما يدفع، وإما فاعل مختار أو موجب مع شرط حادث فيلزم أن يكون له أثر ليصح أنه مؤثر إذ حيث لا أثر لا تأثير، والعدم نفي محض، لا يصلح أثراً
Second: if it persisted, its removal would be impossible, and the consequent is obviously false. The aspect of entailment: that if its removal were possible after persistence, then its removal would be an event needing a cause. Its cause is either its own essence, then its existence would be impossible necessarily, since that whose non-existence is required by its essence would not exist at all. Or the removal of a condition of existence, then the discussion shifts to the removal of that condition, and it leads to infinite regress necessarily, because there would be a condition for its removal, and so on. Or the occurrence of an opposite, and that is false for two reasons: One: circularity, because the occurrence of one of two opposites in a substrate is conditional on the removal of the other, which depends on it. So if the removal of the other depends on its occurrence, it would be circular. Second: opposition and mutual exclusion are from both sides, so the repulsion of the arising one by the persistent is not more likely than the repulsion of the persistent by it; rather repulsion is easier than removal, because in removal there is a power of stability and a precedence of fixity that is not in repulsion. Or a free agent or a necessitating cause with a condition that arises, then it would follow that it has an effect so that it is effective, since where there is no effect, there is no influence. And non-existence is pure negation and is not suitable as an effect.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the persistence of accidents. It argues that if an accident persisted, its removal would be impossible. The proof examines all possible causes for removal. It cannot be due to its own essence, because if non-existence were inherent to it, it would never have existed. It cannot be due to removal of a condition, because that leads to infinite regress. It cannot be due to an opposite arising, because that would involve circularity (both opposites mutually depend on each other's removal) and because the repulsion of the opposite is symmetrical. It cannot be due to an agent with an effect, because non-existence is pure negation and cannot be an effect. Thus, removal is impossible, contradicting the observed fact that accidents do cease.

Key Terms
زوال
ضد
دور
فاعل مختار
أثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An accident can be removed by an agent causing non-existence.
Correction: Non-existence is pure negation and cannot be an effect; an agent requires a positive effect to have influence.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof reject the possibility that the accident's removal is caused by its own essence?
A) Because an essence cannot be a cause of its own non-existence.
B) Because if non-existence were essential to it, it would never have existed.
C) Because the essence is eternal.
D) Because the accident cannot be self-caused.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of removing a persistent accident demonstrates that accidents cannot endure, as their observed cessation would be impossible if they did.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You've just read a proof that the removal of a persistent accident is impossible. Yet you experience change every moment. How does this truth expose your hidden assumption that you can change yourself without Allah? Will you today turn to Him as the only One who can remove your faults?
?
The proof shows that non-existence cannot be an effect of an agent. Reflect on a time you tried to eliminate a sin through your own effort alone. Did you truly succeed, or did you end up frustrated? What would it mean for you to rely entirely on Allah's power to remove that sin?
?
Consider the infinite regress argument. Are there areas in your life where you keep looking for secondary causes—people, circumstances, your own will—instead of the First Cause? What concrete step will you take today to acknowledge that every change, including the removal of your bad habits, comes directly from Allah?

Responding to the Second Proof: Naqd and Hall
Refutation of second proof via naqd and hall, with sectarian perspectives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the counterexample (naqd) and solution (hall) are used to refute the second proof, and appreciate the different positions of Mu'tazila, the author's school, and others.

Core Idea
A valid proof must survive counterexample – bodies cease, so the proof fails.

In this passage, the author refutes the second proof using three classical methods – counterexample, reversal, and solution – but only the first two are fully developed. The counterexample shows that the same reasoning would imply bodies cannot cease, but they do; the solution then resolves the issue by distinguishing between substance and accident persistence. This lesson will walk you through the naqd and hall arguments, and clarify the various sectarian responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source text carefully. Note the three methods announced at the beginning: naqd, qalb, and hall. The passage develops naqd and hall; qalb is cut off.
Pay attention to the technical terms: نقض, قلب, حل.
2
Analyze the counterexample (naqd)
The author states that if the second proof were valid, bodies would not be able to cease – but we know they do cease. This is the core naqd. Then different deflections are presented: al-Nazzam denies persistence of bodies, Karramiyya allow cessation, some philosophers say persistence is necessary, etc.
The naqd works by showing the proof proves too much.
3
Understand the solution (hall) according to different schools
The Mu'tazila argue that a body ceases when God creates an accident of annihilation (fana’) in it. The author’s school (likely Ash’ari) says the substance’s persistence depends on an accident; if God stops creating that accident, the substance ceases. This solves the problem without needing an accident in an accident.
The text says 'عندنا' – refer to the author's own school.
4
Examine the objection and response about accidents
An objection asks: if annihilation is an accident, how can it subsist in another accident? The answer distinguishes between substance (suitable substrate) and accident (not suitable). This ties to the debate over the existence of non-existent (ma‘dum).
The response appeals to the Mu'tazilite principle of the existence of non-existent.
5
Note the incompleteness of the qalb argument
The passage ends with 'فلأن' indicating the beginning of the qalb (reversal) argument, which is not completed in the available text. Recognize that the refutation would have included this third method.
This is a manuscript limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد بالنقض والقلب والحل أما النقض فتقريره أنه لو صح هذا الدليل لزم أن لا يكون الأجسام باقية وإلا لما جاز عدمها بعين ما ذكر، ودفعه بالمناقشة في بقائها كما نسب إلى النظام، أو في جواز زوالها كما نسب إلى الكرامية، وبعض الفلاسفة يندفع بأن الأول ضروري، والثاني مبين في بابه. نعم يدفع عند المعتزلة بأن زوال الجسم يكون بأن يخلق الله تعالى فيه عرضاً منافياً للبقاء هو الفناء، وعندنا بأن ذات الجوهر وإن كان شرطاً للعرض، إلا أن بقاءه مشروط بالعرض، فيجوز أن ينعدم بأن ينقطع تجدد ما لزمه من العرض بأن لا يخلقه الله تعالى ولا يصح هذا في العرض لأنه لا يصلح محلاً للعرض حتى يقوم به عرض الفناء، أو الذي هو شرط البقاء. فإن قيل : قيام العرض بالعرض ليس بأبعد من قيام العرض بالمعدوم . قلنا : مبنى على أصلهم في ثبوت المعدوم، فإن كان جوهراً يصلح محلاً للعرض، وإن كان عرضاً فلا كما في حال الموجود، وأما القلب فلأن
And it was rebutted by counterexample (naqd), reversal (qalb), and solution (hall). As for the counterexample, its formulation: that if this proof were valid, it would follow that bodies would not be persistent, otherwise their non-existence would not be possible by the same reasoning. And it is deflected by disputing their persistence, as attributed to al-Nazzam, or by disputing the possibility of their removal, as attributed to the Karramiyya. And some philosophers deflect by saying the first is necessary and the second is explained in its place. Indeed, according to the Mu'tazila, it is deflected by saying that the removal of the body occurs by God creating in it an accident opposing persistence, which is annihilation (fana'). And according to us, even though the essence of the substance is a condition for the accident, its persistence is conditional on the accident, so it is possible that it ceases by the cessation of the renewal of what necessarily accompanies it from the accident, by God not creating it. And this is not valid for the accident because it is not suitable as a substrate for an accident such that the accident of annihilation or that which is the condition of persistence subsists in it. If it is said: the subsistence of an accident in an accident is not more improbable than the subsistence of an accident in a non-existent. We say: it is based on their principle of the existence of the non-existent. If it is a substance, it is suitable as a substrate for an accident; if it is an accident, then no, just as in the case of the existent. As for the reversal, because...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage provides the response to the second proof using three methods: counterexample (naqd), reversal (qalb), and solution (hall). The counterexample shows that the same reasoning would apply to bodies, which are generally considered persistent yet can cease. Various objections are considered: al-Nazzam denied persistence of bodies, the Karramiyya allowed their removal. The Mu'tazila explain removal by God creating a contrary accident of annihilation. The author's own school (Ash'ari?) explains that the substance's persistence depends on the accident, so the substance can cease when God does not renew the accident. Then an objection is raised: if an accident cannot subsist in an accident, how can annihilation be an accident in a body? The answer distinguishes between substance and accident as substrate. The passage ends with the beginning of the reversal argument, which is cut off.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second proof is irrefutable and shows that accidents cannot cease.
Correction: The proof fails because the same reasoning would apply to bodies, which do cease. This counterexample invalidates the proof.

Checkpoint
What is the main counterexample (naqd) used against the second proof?
A) That accidents can subsist in accidents.
B) That bodies are not persistent.
C) That the same reasoning would imply bodies cannot cease, but they do.
D) That the proof relies on circular reasoning.

Key Takeaway
The second proof is refuted by showing its logic leads to a contradiction when applied to bodies, and the solution lies in understanding how substances depend on accidents for persistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Ash'ari position teaches that your very persistence from moment to moment depends on God's continuous creation of accidents. Do you live with that awareness—that without His constant will, you would cease to exist? Or do you unconsciously assume you are self-sustaining? Commit to reminding yourself daily: 'I exist because He wills it now.'
?
Different Islamic schools offered different solutions to the same theological problem. When you encounter differing views among Muslims on matters of creed, do you engage with their reasoning as the author does here, or do you dismiss them? Will you take the time to understand the arguments of those you disagree with, even if you ultimately differ?

The Naqd Refutation and Theological Responses
The author refutes an argument by counterexample, involving persistence of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the naqd (counterexample) argument and how different theological schools defend against it in the context of body persistence.

Core Idea
A counterexample (naqd) exposes an absurd consequence to refute an opponent's proof.

This passage presents a powerful dialectical tool: the counterexample (naqd). The author demonstrates how a proof is turned against itself by showing it leads to an unacceptable conclusion. We will follow the argument, explore how al-Nazzam, the Karramiyya, philosophers, Mu'tazila, and Ash'aris each respond, and then examine an objection about accidents subsisting in accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Naqd Structure
Read the first sentence. Notice how the naqd is formulated: if the proof were sound, then an absurd consequence would follow. This is the basic form of a counterexample refutation.
The naqd here targets a proof about the possibility of bodies ceasing to exist.
2
Explore the Refutations
The passage then lists several refutations of the naqd: disputing endurance (al-Nazzam), disputing cessation (Karramiyya), claiming premises are necessary (philosophers). Understand each response.
The author does not endorse these views; he presents them as possible rebuttals.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite and Ash'arite Positions
The Mu'tazila explain cessation via an accident of annihilation. The Ash'aris argue that substance endurance depends on a sustaining accident, so cessation occurs when God stops renewing it. Compare the two.
This is a key difference in theories of causality and persistence.
4
Analyze the Objection and Response
An objector says: an accident subsisting in an accident is no stranger than an accident in a non-existent. The author replies based on the principle of thubut al-ma'dum (existence of the non-existent). Grasp the dialectic.
This touches on ontology and the nature of accidents and substances.
5
Reflect on the Overall Argument
Consider how the passage exemplifies kalam's method: presenting a proof, then a counterexample, then multiple responses, then a further objection and answer. This layered dialectic is typical.
Notice that the author does not settle on one view; he presents the range of opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض فتقريره أنه لو صح هذا الدليل لزم أن لا يكون الأجسام باقية وإلا لما جاز عدمها بعين ما ذكر، ودفعه بالمناقشة في بقائها كما نسب إلى النظام، أو في جواز زوالها كما نسب إلى الكرامية، وبعض الفلاسفة يندفع بأن الأول ضروري، والثاني مبين في بابه. نعم يدفع عند المعتزلة بأن زوال الجسم يكون بأن يخلق الله تعالى فيه عرضاً منافياً للبقاء هو الفناء، وعندنا بأن ذات الجوهر وإن كان شرطاً للعرض، إلا أن بقاءه مشروط بالعرض، فيجوز أن ينعدم بأن ينقطع تجدد ما لزمه من العرض بأن لا يخلقه الله تعالى ولا يصح هذا في العرض لأنه لا يصلح محلاً للعرض حتى يقوم به عرض الفناء، أو الذي هو شرط البقاء. فإن قيل : قيام العرض بالعرض ليس بأبعد من قيام العرض بالمعدوم . قلنا : مبنى على أصلهم في ثبوت المعدوم، فإن كان جوهراً يصلح محلاً للعرض، وإن كان عرضاً فلا كما في حال الموجود.
And as for the counterexample (naqd): Its formulation is that if this proof were sound, it would necessarily follow that bodies are not enduring, otherwise it would not be possible for them to cease to exist in the very same way mentioned. And it is refuted by disputing their endurance, as attributed to al-Nazzam, or by disputing the possibility of their cessation, as attributed to the Karramiyya. And some philosophers [say] it is refuted because the first [premise] is necessary, and the second is clarified in its proper place. Rather, according to the Mu'tazila, it is refuted because the cessation of a body occurs by God creating in it an accident contrary to endurance, which is annihilation. And according to us (the Ash'aris), the substance itself, although it is a condition for the accident, yet its endurance is conditioned by the accident. So it is possible for it to cease to exist by the cessation of the renewal of what is necessary for it from the accident, by God not creating it. And this is not valid for the accident because it is not suitable to be a locus for an accident, such that the accident of annihilation or that which is the condition of endurance could subsist in it. If it is said: The subsistence of an accident in an accident is not more improbable than the subsistence of an accident in a non-existent. We say: It is based on their principle regarding the existence of the non-existent (thubut al-ma'dum). For if it is a substance, it is suitable as a locus for an accident, but if it is an accident, it is not, just as in the case of the existent.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the refutation by counterexample (naqd) against an opponent's argument. The author first states that if the proof were correct, it would imply that bodies do not endure, which is absurd. Then he mentions ways the proof is refuted: by disputing the persistence of bodies (as al-Nazzam does) or by disputing the possibility of their cessation (as the Karramiyya do). Some philosophers say the first premise is necessary and the second is clarified elsewhere. The author then explains the Mu'tazilite view that God creates an accident of annihilation in the body, and the Ash'arite view that a substance's endurance depends on the accident, so it can cease if God stops creating the accident. He also addresses an objection about whether an accident can subsist in an accident, replying based on the principle of the existence of the non-existent.

Key Terms
نقض (naqd)
بقاء (baqa')
فناء (fana')
جوهر (jawhar)
عرض ('arad)
محل (mahall)
ثبوت المعدوم (thubut al-ma'dum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The naqd is a logical fallacy that disproves the opponent's argument outright.
Correction: In kalam, naqd is a constructive dialectical tool: it shows that the opponent's proof leads to an absurd consequence, but the opponent can respond by disputing premises or offering alternative explanations.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arite position in this passage, how can a body cease to exist?
A) By God creating an accident of annihilation in it.
B) By the body's own nature necessitating non-existence.
C) By the cessation of the renewal of the accident that conditions its endurance, due to God not creating it.
D) By the occurrence of a contrary accident through a created agent.

Key Takeaway
The naqd is a central kalam method for testing proofs, and the diversity of responses shows the richness of theological debate about causality and persistence.

The First Solution: Denying Self-Caused Annihilation Implies Impossibility
The author denies that self-caused annihilation implies impossibility of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility of a thing causing its own cessation in a specific state without making its essence impossible.

Core Idea
Self-caused cessation does not imply essential impossibility if it is conditional.

The author now offers a direct rebuttal to the opponent's disjunctive proof. He attacks one of its premises: the assumption that if something could annihilate itself, it would be impossible in its essence. Here he explains why that inference is flawed—because necessity can be restricted to a particular state, as with motion which perishes after acting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author opens with a denial ('We do not grant') and then qualifies the conditional.
The Arabic uses 'لا نسلم' (we do not grant) as a standard dialectical move.
2
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between essence necessitating non-existence absolutely vs. only in a certain state. Understand why absolute necessity would be problematic but conditional necessity is not.
Think about why motion can cease after existence without being impossible from the start.
3
Reflect on the example of motion
Motion is used as an analogy: it requires cessation after it has occurred, but it does not require non-existence before or during its action. This shows that self-caused cessation can be limited in time.
The example illustrates how a thing may 'necessitate' non-existence only after a period of existence.
4
Connect to the overall argument
This is the first of several solutions. It undermines the opponent's proof by denying that self-caused annihilation necessarily leads to impossibility. Realize that the debate hinges on whether the necessity is absolute or conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحل فيمنع بعض مقدمات بيان إبطال أجزاء المنفصلة، وذلك من وجوه. الأول: لا نسلم أنه لو كان زواله بنفسه لكان ممتنع الوجود، وإنما يلزم لو اقتضى ذاته العدم مطلقاً، وأما إذا اقتضاه في بعض الأحوال كحال ما بعد البقاء فلا، وذلك كالحركة تقتضي العدم عقيب الوجود، غاية الأمر أن ترجح بعض الأوقات للزوال يفتقر إلى شرط لئلا يلزم تخلف المعلول عن تمام العلة.
And as for the solution (al-hall): It denies some premises of the demonstration invalidating the parts of the disjunction. And that is in several ways. The first: We do not grant that if its cessation were by itself, it would be impossible of existence. That would only follow if its essence necessitated non-existence absolutely. But if it necessitates it in some states, like the state after endurance, then no. For example, motion necessitates non-existence after existence, except that the preference of some times for cessation requires a condition to avoid the consequent lagging behind the complete cause.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is the first of the three solutions (hall) that deny certain premises of the opponent's disjunctive proof. The author argues against the idea that if something ceases by itself, it must be impossible in its essence. He says that something can necessitate non-existence only in a particular state (e.g., after endurance), not absolutely. He gives the example of motion, which requires cessation after existence but only under certain conditions. Thus self-caused annihilation does not entail essential impossibility.

Key Terms
حل (hall)
مقدمات (muqaddimat)
إبطال (ibtal)
منفصلة (munfasila)
زوال (zawal)
وجود (wujud)
علة ('illa)
ترجيح (tarjih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'self-caused annihilation' means the essence causes its own non-existence in all circumstances, making the thing impossible from the start.
Correction: The author clarifies that self-caused annihilation can be limited to a specific state (e.g., after endurance), which does not contradict the thing's existence during other states.

Checkpoint
According to the first solution, when does a thing's essence necessitating non-existence not make it impossible?
A) When it necessitates non-existence absolutely.
B) When it necessitates non-existence in all states.
C) When it necessitates non-existence only in some states, like after endurance.
D) When it necessitates non-existence before existence.

Key Takeaway
Self-caused annihilation is rationally possible if the essence requires cessation only in a particular state, like motion after existing, thereby avoiding essential impossibility.

Second Solution: Denying Circularity Through Alternating Conditions
The author denies that removal of condition leads to circularity or infinite regress by positing a finite chain of alternating conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a chain of alternating conditions to avoid circularity and infinite regress in explaining the cessation of accidents.

Core Idea
Cessation by removal of condition avoids circularity when conditions alternate in a finite series.

This passage presents the second solution to a potential objection. The objector claimed that if an accident ceases because a condition is removed, that leads to circularity (what is the condition for the condition?) or infinite regress. The author denies this, offering a model of alternating conditions that ends without a substitute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'condition' (shart), 'accident' ('arad), and 'substitute' (badal).
The passage is short but dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the objection being answered
Understand that the objection claims that removal of condition leads to circularity or infinite regress. The author denies this (lā nusallim).
3
Understand the alternating condition model
The author posits that an accident's existence depends on a series of conditions that are renewed on an alternating basis. Each later condition replaces an earlier one. The series ends at an accident for which the agent produces no substitute. When that final condition is removed, the original accident ceases.
This finite chain avoids both circularity (since conditions are distinct and non-repeating) and infinite regress (since the chain ends).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: لا نسلم أنه لو كان زواله بزوال شرط لزم الدور أو التسلسل لجواز أن يكون وجود العرض مشروطاً بوجود أعراض تتجدد في محالها على سبيل التبادل بأن يصير لاحقاً بدلاً عن سابق في الشرطية إلى أن ينتهي تلاحقهما إلى عرض لا يوجد الفاعل له بدلاً، فحينئذ يزول العرض المشروط بهذا الشرط بزوال شرط.
And the second: We do not grant that if its cessation were by the removal of a condition, then circularity or infinite regress would occur, because it is possible that the existence of the accident is conditioned by the existence of accidents that are renewed in their loci on the basis of alternation, such that the later becomes a substitute for the earlier in conditionality, until their succession ends at an accident for which the agent does not produce a substitute. Then the accident conditioned by this condition ceases by the removal of the condition.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This second solution denies that removal of a condition necessarily leads to circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The author proposes a model where the accident's existence depends on a series of alternating conditions: one condition is replaced by another, and this chain ends at an accident for which no substitute is created. When that final condition is removed, the original accident ceases. This avoids both circularity and infinite regress because the series is finite and non-repeating.

Key Terms
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
شرط (shart)
بدل (badal)
عرض ('arad)
فاعل (fa'il)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that removal of condition always leads to circularity or infinite regress.
Correction: The author shows that with a finite alternating chain of conditions (where the endpoint has no substitute), circularity and infinite regress are avoided.

Checkpoint
How does the second solution avoid circularity when cessation occurs by removal of a condition?
A) By positing a single eternal condition for the accident.
B) By having the condition be the accident itself, creating a loop.
C) By a series of alternating conditions that ends with an accident without a substitute.
D) By arguing that removal of condition is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The second solution shows that a finite alternating chain of conditions avoids circularity and infinite regress in explaining cessation by removal of condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a pattern in your life that you struggle to break—a habit, a fear, or a dependency. Can you identify the conditions that keep it alive? Are you constantly replacing one condition with another, like the alternating series? What would it look like for you to let the chain end by not providing a substitute, trusting that God can remove it directly?
?
When you feel your faith is conditional on certain outcomes (e.g., prayers answered, circumstances improving), do you fall into a cycle of waiting for those conditions to be met? How does the lesson's teaching that conditions can be finite and end with God's direct action free you from that endless waiting?
?
Have you ever justified a sin by convincing yourself that you need a certain condition to change first? If the chain of conditions can be broken at any point by God's will, what is stopping you from seeking that removal today?

intermediate
6 min
The persistence of accidents is like the persistence of bodies
Al-Taftazani states the correct view on accident persistence and establishes rational parity with bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that some accidents, especially those subsisting in the soul, persist necessarily just as bodies do, and that this is known by rational necessity rather than by observation or analogy.

Core Idea
Some accidents persist necessarily, just like bodies.

Al-Taftazani opens by asserting the correct view on the persistence of accidents. He directly compares the persistence of certain accidents—particularly those that subsist in the soul—to the persistence of bodies, and declares that this is a matter of necessary knowledge. This sets the stage for the arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Taftazani states the truth and immediately compares accidents to bodies.
The passage is from 'قال : والحق أن بقاء العرض...' to '...فإن كان هذا ضرورياً فكذا ذاك، وإن كان ذاك باطلاً فكذا هذا'.
2
Identify the main claim
The main claim is that the persistence of an accident in general is like the persistence of a body. Al-Taftazani emphasizes accidents that subsist in the soul, such as knowledge and perceptions.
3
Understand the nature of the knowledge
Al-Taftazani says this knowledge is akin to knowing the persistence of some bodies—if that is necessary, this is also necessary; if that is false, this is false. He is presenting a parity argument.
This is not based on observation or analogy, but on a direct rational insight.
4
Reflect on the Ash‘arite context
The author notes that the absolute denial of accident persistence is an Ash‘arite doctrine, but he argues that the truth is that some accidents do persist.
This shows that not all Ash‘arites agreed on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والحق أن بقاء العرض في الجملة كبقاء الجسم لا سيما الأعراض القائمة بالنفس وليس التعويل على مجرد المشاهدة إذ الأمثال المتواردة قد تشاهد أمراً مستمراً كالماء المصبوب من الأنبوب . والحق يريد أن امتناع وبقاء الأعراض على الإطلاق وإن كان مذهباً للأشاعرة وعليه يبتنى كثير من مطالبهم إلا أن الحق أن العلم ببقاء بعض الأعراض من الألوان والأشكال سيما الأعراض القائمة بالنفس كالعلوم والإدراكات وكثير من الملكات بمنزلة العلم ببقاء بعض الأجسام من غير تفرقة، فإن كان هذا ضرورياً فكذا ذاك، وإن كان ذاك باطلاً فكذا هذا، وليس التعويل في بقاء الأعراض على مجرد المشاهدة، أو على قياسها على الأجسام حتى يرد الاعتراض بأن الأمثال المتجددة على الاستمرار قد يشاهد أمراً مستمراً باقياً كالماء المصبوب من الأنبوب وبأن القياس على الجسم تمثيل بلا جامع ولا على أنه لما جاز وجود العرض في الزمان الثاني بطريق الإعادة مع تخلل العدم فبدونه أولى لأنه ممنوع بمقدمتيه أعني الملازمة، ووضع الملزوم ، كما أن التعويل في بقاء الأجسام ليس على المشاهدة، أو الاستدلال بأنه لولاه لبطل الموت والحياة بناء على أن الحياة عبارة عن استمرار وجود الحيوان والموت عن زوالها لجواز أن تكون الحياة تجدد الأمثال على الاستمرار والموت انقطاعه.
He said: And the truth is that the persistence of the accident in general is like the persistence of the body, especially accidents subsisting in the soul, and reliance is not on mere observation, for successive similar things may be observed as a continuous thing, like water poured from a pipe. And the truth is that the impossibility and persistence of accidents absolutely, although it is a doctrine of the Ash‘arites and many of their demands are built upon it, yet the truth is that knowledge of the persistence of some accidents—like colors and shapes, especially accidents subsisting in the soul, such as knowledge, perceptions, and many dispositions—is akin to knowledge of the persistence of some bodies without distinction. If this is necessary, then so is that; if that is false, then so is this. Reliance in the persistence of accidents is not on mere observation, or on analogy to bodies, such that the objection might be raised that successive similar things may be observed as a continuous existing thing, like water poured from a pipe, and that analogy to a body is a representation without a common criterion, nor on the ground that since the existence of the accident in the second instant is permissible by way of renewal with the interposition of non-existence, then without it is more fitting, because this is rejected in both its premises, namely the implication and the placing of the antecedent. Likewise, reliance in the persistence of bodies is not on observation or on the argument that without it death and life would be invalid, based on the idea that life is the continuity of the existence of the animal and death is its cessation, because it is possible that life is the renewal of similar things continuously and death is its interruption.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents the correct view on the persistence of accidents (بقاء العرض) in the context of Ash‘arite theology. The author begins by stating the truth: the persistence of an accident in general is like the persistence of a body, especially accidents that subsist in the soul (الأعراض القائمة بالنفس) such as knowledge, perceptions, and dispositions. He emphasizes that this persistence is not based on mere observation (المشاهدة), because even a continuous stream of water, which appears persistent, is actually a succession of similar instances. The author then clarifies that although Ash‘arites hold that accidents do not persist absolutely, the truth is that knowledge of the persistence of some accidents (e.g., colors, shapes, mental states) is equivalent to knowledge of the persistence of some bodies—there is no difference. If one is known necessarily, the other is; if one is false, the other is. He rejects objections that rely on observation or analogy to bodies, noting that such analogy lacks a common basis. He also refutes the argument that if an accident can exist in a second moment after a gap, then uninterrupted existence is more fitting, by denying its premises. Finally, he addresses the argument from life and death: life is not necessarily a continuous state but could be a constant renewal of similar instances, and death its interruption. Thus, the persistence of bodies cannot be proven by this argument either.

Key Terms
بقاء العرض
الأعراض القائمة بالنفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All accidents cannot persist at all (the Ash‘arite position).
Correction: Some accidents, especially those subsisting in the soul, do persist just like bodies, and this is known by rational necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for knowing the persistence of some accidents like knowledge and perceptions?
A) Direct sensory observation of their continuity
B) Analogy to the persistence of bodies, which is known by observation
C) It is known necessarily, akin to knowing the persistence of some bodies
D) The argument from life and death

Key Takeaway
Some accidents persist necessarily, just as bodies do, and this knowledge is rational, not empirical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your faith have you been relying on observable evidence rather than accepting what reason and revelation make clear? How does this lesson challenge you to shift your basis of certainty?
?
The passage says knowledge of the persistence of some accidents is akin to knowledge of the persistence of bodies. What persistent quality in your own soul—like knowledge or intention—do you take for granted? Could it be that you assume its persistence from habit? Commit to examining one such quality today.
?
If the Ash‘arite view of total non-persistence were true, every moment of your existence would be a fresh creation by God. Does that thought make you uneasy or grateful? How does your answer reveal your attachment to cause-and-effect thinking?

Refuting objections to accident persistence
Al-Taftazani refutes four objections against accident persistence: from observation, analogy, re-creation, and life/death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes objections based on observation, analogy, re-creation, and the life/death argument, showing that none of these undermine the rational persistence of accidents.

Core Idea
Observation and analogy cannot disprove the persistence of accidents.

After establishing the correct view, al-Taftazani anticipates and refutes several objections that might be raised against the persistence of accidents. He argues that reliance on observation is deceptive, analogy to bodies is invalid, and arguments from re-creation or from life and death are unsound. This lesson unpacks his dialectical responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'وليس التعويل في بقاء الأعراض...' to the end, along with the translation.
This part contains the refutations.
2
Identify the first objection and its refutation
The first objection is that observation shows a continuous stream (like water) is actually successive instances. Al-Taftazani says this is exactly why observation cannot be relied upon.
3
Analyze the analogy objection
The second objection is that analogizing accidents to bodies lacks a common criterion. Al-Taftazani rejects the analogy as invalid.
4
Examine the re-creation objection
The third objection argues that if an accident can exist after a gap, then uninterrupted existence is more fitting. Al-Taftazani denies its premises.
5
Understand the life/death counter
Finally, he counters the argument from life and death: life need not be a continuous state but could be constant renewal.
Thus bodies also might not persist in a strict sense, but this does not disprove accident persistence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والحق أن بقاء العرض في الجملة كبقاء الجسم لا سيما الأعراض القائمة بالنفس وليس التعويل على مجرد المشاهدة إذ الأمثال المتواردة قد تشاهد أمراً مستمراً كالماء المصبوب من الأنبوب . والحق يريد أن امتناع وبقاء الأعراض على الإطلاق وإن كان مذهباً للأشاعرة وعليه يبتنى كثير من مطالبهم إلا أن الحق أن العلم ببقاء بعض الأعراض من الألوان والأشكال سيما الأعراض القائمة بالنفس كالعلوم والإدراكات وكثير من الملكات بمنزلة العلم ببقاء بعض الأجسام من غير تفرقة، فإن كان هذا ضرورياً فكذا ذاك، وإن كان ذاك باطلاً فكذا هذا، وليس التعويل في بقاء الأعراض على مجرد المشاهدة، أو على قياسها على الأجسام حتى يرد الاعتراض بأن الأمثال المتجددة على الاستمرار قد يشاهد أمراً مستمراً باقياً كالماء المصبوب من الأنبوب وبأن القياس على الجسم تمثيل بلا جامع ولا على أنه لما جاز وجود العرض في الزمان الثاني بطريق الإعادة مع تخلل العدم فبدونه أولى لأنه ممنوع بمقدمتيه أعني الملازمة، ووضع الملزوم ، كما أن التعويل في بقاء الأجسام ليس على المشاهدة، أو الاستدلال بأنه لولاه لبطل الموت والحياة بناء على أن الحياة عبارة عن استمرار وجود الحيوان والموت عن زوالها لجواز أن تكون الحياة تجدد الأمثال على الاستمرار والموت انقطاعه.
He said: And the truth is that the persistence of the accident in general is like the persistence of the body, especially accidents subsisting in the soul, and reliance is not on mere observation, for successive similar things may be observed as a continuous thing, like water poured from a pipe. And the truth is that the impossibility and persistence of accidents absolutely, although it is a doctrine of the Ash‘arites and many of their demands are built upon it, yet the truth is that knowledge of the persistence of some accidents—like colors and shapes, especially accidents subsisting in the soul, such as knowledge, perceptions, and many dispositions—is akin to knowledge of the persistence of some bodies without distinction. If this is necessary, then so is that; if that is false, then so is this. Reliance in the persistence of accidents is not on mere observation, or on analogy to bodies, such that the objection might be raised that successive similar things may be observed as a continuous existing thing, like water poured from a pipe, and that analogy to a body is a representation without a common criterion, nor on the ground that since the existence of the accident in the second instant is permissible by way of renewal with the interposition of non-existence, then without it is more fitting, because this is rejected in both its premises, namely the implication and the placing of the antecedent. Likewise, reliance in the persistence of bodies is not on observation or on the argument that without it death and life would be invalid, based on the idea that life is the continuity of the existence of the animal and death is its cessation, because it is possible that life is the renewal of similar things continuously and death is its interruption.

Commentary
This passage presents the correct view on the persistence of accidents (بقاء العرض) in the context of Ash‘arite theology. The author begins by stating the truth: the persistence of an accident in general is like the persistence of a body, especially accidents that subsist in the soul (الأعراض القائمة بالنفس) such as knowledge, perceptions, and dispositions. He emphasizes that this persistence is not based on mere observation (المشاهدة), because even a continuous stream of water, which appears persistent, is actually a succession of similar instances. The author then clarifies that although Ash‘arites hold that accidents do not persist absolutely, the truth is that knowledge of the persistence of some accidents (e.g., colors, shapes, mental states) is equivalent to knowledge of the persistence of some bodies—there is no difference. If one is known necessarily, the other is; if one is false, the other is. He rejects objections that rely on observation or analogy to bodies, noting that such analogy lacks a common basis. He also refutes the argument that if an accident can exist in a second moment after a gap, then uninterrupted existence is more fitting, by denying its premises. Finally, he addresses the argument from life and death: life is not necessarily a continuous state but could be a constant renewal of similar instances, and death its interruption. Thus, the persistence of bodies cannot be proven by this argument either.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The persistence of accidents can be proven or disproven by direct observation or simple analogy.
Correction: Observation is unreliable (e.g., water from a pipe), analogy lacks a common basis, and other arguments are unsound.

B) He points out that what is observed may be a succession of similar instances, not genuine persistence
C) He says that observation only applies to bodies, not accidents
D) He claims that accidents are invisible and cannot be observed

Key Takeaway
None of the common objections—observation, analogy, re-creation, or life/death—successfully disprove the persistence of some accidents.

?

Will you begin to see every moment as a new gift from Him, not a continuation of the old?
?

?

This passage exposes how even seemingly strong arguments (like life/death proving persistence) can be dismantled. How humble are you before the scholars who have thought more deeply? Will you commit to studying these issues with the same rigor?

Refuting Objections on Persistence
Refutes arguments against persistence of accidents and bodies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes objections against the persistence of accidents and bodies by showing that observation and analogy are insufficient, and that logical necessity is required.

Core Idea
Persistence is proven by logical necessity, not observation or analogy.

In this passage, al-Taftazani addresses objections raised against the doctrine of persistence (baqā') of accidents and bodies. He argues that critics mistakenly rely on empirical observation or analogical reasoning. Instead, the argument for persistence rests on a deeper logical foundation. Let's follow his refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the initial distinction
The author begins by setting up a parallel: if one position is necessary, the other is likewise; if one is false, the other is likewise. This frames the refutation symmetrically.
2
Analyze the refutation on accidents
The author denies that persistence of accidents relies on observation (mushāhada) or analogy (qiyās) to bodies. He dismisses the objection that water flowing from a pipe appears persistent, noting that continuous renewal can create an illusion of persistence.
Also reject the argument based on 'renewal after non-existence being less appropriate than persistence' because its premises are invalid.
3
Examine the refutation on bodies
Similarly, persistence of bodies is not based on observation or on the inference that otherwise life and death would be meaningless. The author notes that life could be continuous renewal of similars, and death their interruption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفرقة، فإن كان هذا ضرورياً فكذا ذاك، وإن كان ذاك باطلاً فكذا هذا، وليس التعويل في بقاء الأعراض على مجرد المشاهدة، أو على قياسها على الأجسام حتى يرد الاعتراض بأن الأمثال المتجددة على الاستمرار قد يشاهد أمراً مستمراً باقياً كالماء المصبوب من الأنبوب وبأن القياس على الجسم تمثيل بلا جامع ولا على أنه لما جاز وجود العرض في الزمان الثاني بطريق الإعادة مع تخلل (۱) العدم فبدونه أولى لأنه ممنوع بمقدمتيه أعني الملازمة، ووضع الملزوم، كما أن التعويل في بقاء الأجسام ليس على المشاهدة (٢)، أو الاستدلال بأنه لولاه لبطل الموت والحياة بناء على أن الحياة عبارة عن استمرار وجود الحيوان والموت عن زوالها لجواز أن تكون الحياة تجدد الأمثال على الاستمرار والموت انقطاعه.
A distinction: if this is necessary, then that is likewise; and if that is false, then this is likewise. Reliance in the persistence of accidents is not on mere observation, nor on measuring them against bodies, such that the objection arises that continuously renewed similars may be observed as a persistent continuous thing, like water poured from a pipe, and that measurement against a body is an analogy without a common ground; nor is it because when the existence of an accident in the second time is allowed by way of renewal with the interposition (1) of non-existence, then without it is more appropriate; for it is prevented by its two premises, namely the implication and the placement of the implied, just as reliance in the persistence of bodies is not on observation (2), nor on the inference that otherwise death and life would be invalid based on life being the continuation of the existence of the living and death being its cessation, because it is possible that life is the renewal of similars continuously and death is their interruption.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of objections against the persistence of accidents and bodies. The author begins by stating a distinction: if one position is necessary, the other is likewise; if one is false, the other is likewise. He then argues that reliance for persistence is not based on mere observation (mushāhada) or on analogy to bodies, otherwise objections would arise: that continuously renewed similars (e.g., water from a pipe) appear persistent, and that analogy is invalid without a common ground. Furthermore, it is not argued that if an accident can exist in a second moment by renewal after non-existence, then persistence without non-existence is better; this is rejected because its premises—the implication and the placement of the implied—are invalid. Similarly, the persistence of bodies is not based on observation or the inference that otherwise life and death would be meaningless, because life could be continuous renewal of similars and death their interruption.

Key Terms
بقاء الأعراض
بقاء الأجسام
المشاهدة
القياس
الأمثال المتجددة
الإعادة
الملازمة
الملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Persistence of accidents and bodies is obvious from everyday experience (e.g., water flowing).
Correction: The author argues that observation alone cannot distinguish genuine persistence from continuous renewal of similars. Logical necessity, not empirical evidence, is the foundation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the objection that observed water flow seems persistent not a valid argument for the persistence of accidents?
A) Because water flow is not an accident.
B) Because observation alone cannot prove persistence due to continuous renewal of similars.
C) Because the objection is based on a false premise about water.
D) Because the author denies any role for observation.

Key Takeaway
Persistence of accidents and bodies cannot be proven by observation or analogy; it requires a logical argument that avoids the pitfalls of continuous renewal.

The author refutes the argument that because a thing can exist after non-existence, its continuous existence is more appropriate. When it comes to your belief in Allah's attributes—like His life, His power—are you content with weak analogies from creation, or are you seeking the rigorous proofs that leave no room for doubt? Are you willing to dismantle the analogies you've relied on and rebuild your certainty on solid logical ground?
